They serve as a relay point along their line of motion; (b) tension (of the material elements); this must be near breaking point (this also determines the proportion of the parts); (c) molecular forces of the material, which must be used in the direction of their greatest compatibility; (d) there must be no excess materials or elements, and no material left over.' The main feature of a construction is that it should not contain surplus materials or elements. The chief distinguishing mark of a composition is hierarchy and subordination. (Inkhuk archive) Extracts from the minutes of Inkhuk’s Working Group of Architects, 1921 ‘The task that faces us is the study of the elements, qualities and properties of architecture. . .. The most important elements in architecture are space, construction, form, and the rest of its elements merely follow these.’ I am surprised that members of the group should still ask questions such as: “Why is the study of space a matter of first priority?".. . The quality of space is architecture’s unique concern, yet architecture does not undertake its study and makes poor use of it.' (Inkhuk archive) Extracts from an article on Foundations for the Construction of a Theory of Architecture (Under the Banner of Rationalist Aesthetics)’, 1926 Architectural rationality, just like technical rationality, is based on an economic principle. The difference between them is that technical rationality is the economy of labour and materials in creating an appropriate structure, while architectural rationality is economy of psychic energy in perception of the structure's spatial and functional properties. A synthesis of these two rationalities within a single structure constitutes Ratio-Architecture. . . . The architect constructs a form, introducing elements which are not technological or utilitarian in the strict sense and may be regarded as “architectural motifs". These “motifs"must be rational in an architectural sense and serve man's highest technological requirement – to find his direction in space. . . . The work of an architect on the geometrical expressiveness of a form, which we always perceive in perspective, consists in getting the image resulting from the perception of real perspective as near as possible to the image conceived in the design. . . . Could-one allow an architect engaged in constructing a form to be ignorant of how it would be perceived by a spectator? Such a dereliction would imply a total lack of principle and the patent lack of any mastery whatsoever in the field of geometrical expressiveness. It is essential to assert that the architectural geometric aspect of a material form should be treated in such a way that the spectator actually sees its geometrical configuration to whatever extent the case concerned demands this.’ (Izvestiya Asnova [Asnova News], Moscow, 1926, pp.3–5) ## From an article entitled The Psycho-Technical Laboratory for Architecture (Posing the Question)’, 1926 ‘The architect must be aware, even if only in an elementary way, of the laws of perception and the means of achieving an impact, so that he may have at his disposal when practising his craft evervthing that modern science can offer. Among the scí- ences which assist in the development of architecture, a serious gap will be filled by the still new science of psycho-technology.... The works that I – and subsequently also my colleagues – have produced in Vkhutemas since 1920 in the realm of architecture have been checked by psycho-technical means and will Chapter 2/The leaders of the new direction help to establish architectural principles on the basis of rationalist aesthetics. The most correct approach to the resolution of this question would be the organization within “Asnova” of a psycho-technical laboratory for the study of questions of rational architecture.... How many misunderstandings in the evaluation of the quality of architectural work, which are attributable to the absence of an agreed terminology, even among specialists, could be removed by such a laboratory!’ (Izvestiya Asnova, Moscow, 1926) Extract from Ladovsky’s preface to Krutikov’s article The Application of the Theory of Union to the Investigation and Measurement of Aptitude for Spatial Composition’, 1929 In establishing those features which define the concept of architect, it was essential first and foremost to define it as the basic feature of the contemporary architect's activity that he organizes the perception of space and spatial forms. In other words, it was necessary to formulate in a concrete and scientific way what is broadly defined nowadays by the concept of an architectural (i.e.spatial) composition.' (Arkhitektura i Vkhutein [Architecture and the Vkhutein], No. 1, January 1929, p. 5) Extract from a lecture given at a conference of Vkhutein graduates, 1929 ‘If an architect is to provide for an individual’s feelings, he must provide for them in such a way that these feelings are expended more economically, that an individual chiefly devotes his feelings to spatial entities, to spatial objects. It is on this particular level that the organization of his psychic energy takes place in accordance with the principle of economy of perception.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura [Contemporary Architecture], 1929, No.4, p. 144) Extract from an article on “Historical” and “Socialist” Moscow’, 1930 The concept of a town’s growth cannot be reduced to mere Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos terms of the mechanical increase of its territory, of the width of its thoroughfares, the height of its buildings etc. Growth must be regarded as organic and representing an organism that is not just quantitatively, but also qualitatively different at various stages in its development. . . . The radial-concentric form of city plan has many supporters in town-planning literature worldwide. It is therefore essential to examine it in greater detail. The medievel fortified town, Howard's garden city, Unwin's system of satellite towns and Le Corbusier's proposals for replanning Paris, all these planning compositions may be included in a single category of static forms, despite the apparent differences in form and purpose between them. . . . The grafting of these systems on to growing and active cities must inevitably provoke unhealthy symptoms in their growth. A specific indication of their mechanical nature is that these systems are only capable of making sense during a minimal space of time if they are fully implemented, while in the very next moment of their growth it will become necessary to destroy them. In short, they do not provide for growth from a “cell" into a system, from a rudimentary system to another more elaborate one etc. While “static" factors predominated over dynamic ones in medieval times, i.e. given a relatively slow tempo of life and an inadequate sense of the passage of time, the radial-concentric system might have been capable of maintaining its place for planning purposes for a while, but, as capitalism developed and grew, it broke down everywhere. . . . it was replaced by the grid system of planning as an expression of fluidity, a sort of ceaseless territorial conveyor belt which was more suited to the needs of capitalism and of mechanical, rather than organic growth. The most extreme expression of this fluidity is the concept of the linear city. These planning structures will inevitably prove to be weak organisms, because they represent an expression of utmost dynamism and reduce three-dimensional space to a single measure by stressing their linear quality. Yet modern material culture and technology make it possible to solve town-planning tasks in three dimensions by stressing “horizontal two-dimensionality". . . . the geometric character of the circular territory reflects its spatially static quality, while the physical nature ofits structure only allows within it an increase in density at best . . . A sectoral system of growth might seem to be feasible in terms of radial-concentric planning, but essential dynamic geometrical considerations conflict with it, inevitably distort it and therefore make it impossible to implement. . . . On the other hand, one must decribe the growth of a city as organic when its growth as a whole makes provision for the growth of its different component organs with their various roles within a single economic time and space system. . . . If one were to assume a total harmonization of form, i.e. if individual rings were to represent different organs – areas designated for particular purposes – the growth of any one of them would spell the decay of another. If, on the other hand, one were to abandon the difference of functional assignment between the various rings and accept their functional uniformity, a centripetal force would develop – all else being equal – on the strength of the economics inherent in the dynamic geometrical principle. This can be represented as an inward pressure of the rings upon each other towards the centre, while the central circle's tendency to grow would thus encounter the vast and insurmountable resistance of the rings. . . . in ring planning . . . the centre, which tends to develop naturally in a horizontal direction, encounters a resistance by the rings which it finds hard to surmount and the resolution of the most fundamental factor in the life of a city – the dialectic process of its growth –is not catered for by building on such a plan, since growth is impossible without causing the collapse of surrounding – and, it must be assumed, equally live – organs of the city. . . . , . . The centre of a city must be able to grow not only in the third dimension, upwards, but equally in a horizontal direction, progressively outwards. Therefore, the centre of a city must consist of an axial line, not a static point. By breaking open the rings and bending them out into the shape of a horseshoe, we shall make it possible for the centre, as well as the corresponding ramifications of the former rings, to grow. The centre will become fan-shaped. Such a shape is more appropriate for a centre since, as the city grows, and as its dynamic increases and its organization becomes more complex, the centre will not be hemmed in, but be able instead to develop within the area of the fan. In this construction, the entire city and its centre represent a kind of stream which gradually widens.' (Stroitelstvo Moskvy [Moscow Construction], 1930, No. 1, pp. 17–20) Extract from an article on The Planning of Avtostroi and Magnitogorsk in a Higher Educational Establishment’ The significance of planning as a separate discipline is only rather slowly being recognized by our architectural community.... . . . In our country, in the era of the building of socialism, architectural associations continue to structure their work from the particular to the general, from the building tothe town, as our foreign colleagues are obliged to do in a capitalist order of society. We cannot regard individual buildings as anything but particles of a city as a whole. Our associations must therefore restructure their organizational work and proceed from city to building, not the other way about. . . . Our builders and ideologists are mistaken when they equate structural with social types of dwellings. Dwellings either identical in social terms, or of a disparate kind, such as individual flats or hostels with different degrees of communalization, may be accommodated in skyscrapers just as well as in any other type of known structure – blocks, axial buildings with spurs or grouped pavilions consisting of separate, spatially discrete bedrooms collected round the communal accommodation in a complex cluster. It should be stressed that a dwelling for a more developed social order can be the more easily accommodated in buildings of diverse structural kinds, although differences would, of course, arise. But any such a difference would involve slight nuances in the pattern of daily life, rather than a difference in the central social aims pursued. . . . A worker's life and habits are far more complicated in their structure than might appear at first sight. Differences in trade, age, physique, psychology, senility, disablement etc, all require different, rather than identical forms of dwelling. And this implies alternatives, most easily provided by the free combination of all known – or recently invented – types of structure, than by an arid, functionally formalistic, self-imposed limitation and the cramming of the intricate organism of life into unbending frameworks devised by the meagre imagination of a designer or ideologist.' (Sovetskaya arkhitektura [Soviet Architecture], 1931,Nos. 1–2, pp.21–24) Alexander Vesnin (1883–1959) Alexander Alexandrovich Vesnin was the leader of Constructivism in Soviet architecture. He was born and spent his childhood in the ancient Russian town of Yurevets on the Volga. He graduated from the Moscow Practical Academy and entered the Petersburg Institute of Civil Engineers from which he graduated in 1912. With his elder brothers, Leonid and Viktor, he produced a number of architectural projects before the Revolution, several of which were carried out. Those designs which used the tradition of Russian Classicism displayed great mastery, while the Vesnin brothers were less successful when they worked in other styles. Well before the Revolution, Alexander Vesnin became seriously involved in painting, at the same time as he was studying and doing architectural work. In painting, he studied first under Konstantin Yuon and E.Tsionglinsky in 1907–11, and then under Vladimir Tatlin in 1912–13. He painted the ceiling of the Sirotkin House in Nizhny-Novgorod in 1915. During the early Soviet years, Alexander Vesnin worked as a Cubist and abstract painter, and also as a theatrical designer. He took part in the exhibitions of the Left Federation of Artist-Painters and the Trade Union of Artist-Painters, both held in 1918; he showed in the First State Exhibition of Suprematist and Non-Objective Painting in 1919 and in the Constructivist Painters' show, 5×5=25’, in 1921 in Moscow, as well as in a travelling exhibition of painters from the Russian Republic that went to Amsterdam, Venice and Rome in 1922. As a theatrical designer he worked on sets for the Maly Theatre: The Road to Glory and The Marriage of Figaro in 1919, The Government Inspector and sketches for Oliver Cromwell in 1920; for the Kamerny Theatre he did L’Annonce faite à Marie in 1920, sketches for Romeo and Juliet in 1921, Phèdre in 1922 and The Man who was Thursday in 1923; and, for the Children’s Theatre, Adalmina’s Pearl in 1921. He designed the decorations for the Kremlin and Red Square on the first May Day holiday after the Revolution,in 1918, and -jointly with Popova – the mass display on the Khodynskoe Field in Moscow in 1921. He also illustrated books. In 1921, Alexander Vesnin became a professor at Vkhutemas, where he first taught painting and drawing, and later headed an architectural studio. He was active at Inkhuk during 1921–24 and in the Architectural Group of Lef. Alexander Vesnin was permanent chairman of the Con- Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos structivists’ Union of Contemporary Architects, Osa, from 1925 to 1931, and chief editor of their periodical Sovremennaya arkhitektura(Contemporary Architecture). From 1923, he did a great deal of architectural work in collaboration with either one or both of his brothers. Their principal designs during the period 1923–34 were: aPalace of Labour, 1923; the Leningradskaya Pravda and Arkos buildings and an aircraft hangar in 1924; a telegraph office in 1925; a department store at Krasnaya Presna in 1927, which was carried out; the Lenin Library, 1928; Housing Communes for Stalingrad and Kuznetsk in 1929–30; Workers' Clubs in Baku in 1928–32, which were carried out; a theatre in Kharkov, 1930; the Palace of Culture in Moscow in 1931–37, which was carried out; the Palace of Soviets in Moscow, 1931–32; and the People’s Commissariat for Heavy Industry in Moscow, 1934. In 1933, Alexander Vesnin and his brother Viktor took over one of the design studios which were set up at that time by the Moscow Soviet and soon afterwards transferred, first to the People's Commissariat for Heavy Industry, then to the People’s Commissariat for the Oil Industry. During the 1930s, Alexander Vesnin took an active part in aesthetic and professional debates but thereafter gradually withdrew from active contributions to theory and practice. Alexander Vesnin’s writings Credo’– April 1922 ‘The tempo of modern times is quick and dynamic; its rhythm is clear, accurate, straight-lined and mathematical.Materials and efficiency determine the make-up of an object produced by the modern artist. It does not matter whether the object concerned is both efficient and useful – such as an engineering construction or a household article – or merely efficient, such as a laboratory project aimed at resolving problems posed by the new modern form. Every real object produced by the modern artist must play its part in life as an active force to organize the individual’s consciousness, exert a psycho-physiological effect upon him and prompt him to energetic activity. Clearly, objects producd by a modern artist must be pure constructions, freed of all representational ballast and devised in accordance with a direct geometric curve and the principle of economy of maximum effect. Since the construction of any object is a matter of the strong bonding of the main elements of plasticity (material, colour, line, surface, texture . . .) the artist must treat their study as his first priority. I regard all these elements as materialized energy endowed with dynamic qualities (movement, tension, weight, speed . . .) to be efficiently regulatedby the artist. Just as everó part of an engine represents an active force, essential to the functioning of the given system and embodied in the appropriate shape and material, and these may not be wilfully altered without damaging the working of the entire system, so in every object constructed by an artist each component element represents a materialized force and cannot be discarded or altered without impeding the efficient functioning of the given system, i.e. the object. The modern engineer has created works of genius: the bridge, the steam locomotive, the aircraft, the crane. . .. The modern artist must produce objects equal to them in strength, tension and potential, as organizing principles in terms of their psycho-physiological impact on human consciousness.' (Inkhuk archive) Extracts from the minutes of the Subsection of Modern Art, Spi, Rakhn (Spatial Art Section, Russian Academy for Artistic Science), 19 March 1929 Introduction by A.A. Vesnin on “The Tasks of ModernArchitecture" A.A. Vesnin pointed out that the architect must engage in the active construction of the new life and share in the formulation of original architectural requirements, rather than accept their finished statement. The new life demands a new presentation, and this is only possible if new materials and the latest technical data are used. . . . N.I. Brunov asked A.A. Vesnin about the extent to which the architect takes account of sensation and the extent to which architecture is dependent upon technology. A. A. Vesnin explained that the basic task of architecture is to organize a new life, while technology is a means of carrying this out. There can be no organization if antiquated technology is used. As to“sensation", he remarked that it did not enter into the task of architecture, but that it was an inevitable result of any work the conception of which moved the spectator. A functional form, developed to perfection, would necessarily also be perfect for the spectator – as is an aircraft, for instance. The intuitive element would undoubtedly diminish as time went by. N.I. Brunov asked where the borderline lay between what was and was not art, if an aircraft was to be regarded as art. A.A. Vesnin considered the difference to be purely qualitative. .. . The architect's task is to organize space for a particular form of life.' (Iz istorii sovetskoi arkhitektury 1926–1932 gg. Dokumenty i materialy [From the History of Soviet Architecture, 1926–1932. Documents and papers], Moscow, 1970, pp. 113–14) Extracts from a speech made at the debate in the Union of Soviet Architects on the subject of Creative Tasks of Soviet Architecture and the Problem of the Architectural Heritage’, 1933 . .. The lecturer said that Brunelleschi made creative use of Classicism and that we must therefore also make creative use of Classicism, yet we know that Brunelleschi introduced Roman capitals and cornices into architectureunaltered. That was perfectly natural at the time. . . . Nowadays, however, the word“to use" should be chucked out and replaced by the word“to assimilate”, i.e. to understand and surpass. But this certainly does not mean “to appropriate”, as is now said and done, . . . What should one gather from the history of architecture? First and foremost, one should be aware of the essence of architecture, i. e. of that basic feature which is peculiar to architecture. In this, the organization of life and living processes comes first. Architectonics come second, i.e. the laws of structuring space, and one must investigate how these develop and gradually change. . . . Secondly – concerning form and content . . . the vital processes of life enter into architecture, the task of organizing life itself and, on the other hand, so does ideological content. Chapter 2/The leaders of the new direction What therefore is form? It is space so organized in a material that it lends substance to the given content. . . . We are concerned with elements of form to match our content . .. since we incorporate life itself in our content, we need a dialectic understanding of its development, we need to understand it ideologically and artistically. We may in this way approach one of the basic problems of the new architecture. The second lesson that the architectural heritages teaches us is the unity of architecture with technology . . .' (Arkhitektura SSSR [Architecture of the USSR], 1933, Nos.3–4, pp. 14–15) Extracts from an article entitled The Problem of the Interior’, 1934 The plan is the basis and foundation of every architectural design. But we have become accustomed to making no artifical distinctions in design work and to carrying out the processes of analysis and synthesis simultaneously. The consideration of the ground plan, elevation, axonometric and perspective designs all take place in parallel; in other words, an integrated process of creating an architectural image is at work. This method of work has not always been practised. The architects of the old school – indeed many do this even now – divided up their work mechanically. The plan came first, then sections, elevations, a model, axonometry and then, at last, perspective, which often brought to light all the mistakes inherent in that architectural conception. Our own practice has convinced us that an organically integrated synthetic method of design, from initial sketch plans to overall perspectives, is the most rational, economic and artistically fruitful approach. . . . Both Western and Soviet modern architecture have introduced important changes in the field of design work. I have in mind the principle of so-called free planning, when an architect resolves the organization of interior space purely on the strength of the everyday intellectual and social requirements of human beings. . . . Finally, the system of interior organization in accordance with the principle of “free-flowing spaces” produces a certain illusion of compositional variations withina structure, thereby increasing both its artistic expressiveness and its functional efficiency. Using the term “function” or “functionalism” as frequently as I do, I begin to fear that I will be misunderstood. Modern Part HII/Masters and trends; biographies, statements, manifestos Functionalism in architecture has never claimed the two deplorable teachings which their opponents lay at its door. It has never pretended to have invented new principles unprecedented in architectural practice.All true masters and architects of all countries in all ages have always given thought to the efficient resolution of functional tasks. We have also never said that the function of a structure, in the narrow sense of the word, must govern all other architectural considerations, i. e. the unity of composition of its image, its artistic purpose or even its role as a decorative element within the general conception of a street, a square, or a city. We have merely maintained that one must not neglect to the point ofinsensitivity the functional purpose of buildings, since they are being erected for living human beings with practical – even if exceedingly complex – intellectual, social and living requirements. Thus Functionalism is, in essence, no more than the rebirth of the immemorial primacy of function in architecture as opposed to scholastic, decorative academicism, now applied to the significantly enlarged and more complex operational base of modern man – the citizen of a socialist country. . . . (Arkhitektura SSSR, 1934, No.7, pp. 3–4) Extracts from an article entitled Debatable Questions’, 1934 A certain confusion may be observed at present among many Soviet architects . . . Something quite absurd has happened to the assimilation of the architectural heritage. The majority of architects approach the heritage as though it was a layette put by in a trunk. Each one of them pulls whatever he fancies from it and sticks it into his design. . . . The fight against vulgarization often comes down to a fight against serious, simple and shapely architectural Constructivism, and, moreover, what is described as Constructivism has nothing in common with it as a form of architecture. The very great achievements of Soviet architecture . . . and uninspired trash are lumped together and described as“Constructivism", “boxes” and “vulgarization”. Architectural opulence is taken to mean neither opulence of content, nor the opulence of simple, shapely forms, but a meaningless, disjointed piling-on of details. The fight for quality often comes down to a fight for the quality of technical execution, either in reality or on paper, and the quality of the architectural treatment is ignored. . . . Symbolism is often substituted for imagery in architecture.’ (Literaturnaya gazeta [Literary Gazette], No.135 (45), 8 October 1934) Extracts from a speech at a Moscow-wide convention of architects in February 1936 ‘The problem of form is one of the basic elements in every sort of art, while Formalism is a definitely negative phenomenon against which it is essential to struggle. Yet these two concepts are very often confused with each other. . . . We are searching for new forms. This search must start from the content of life and take account of the functional aspect, as well as of perception etc. All this is part of the content . . . The content is not simply a utilitarian function, and functions of an ideological order also have their share in it. . . A few words about simplicity and simplification or vulgarization. These two concepts are often confused. Simplicity is a great achievement, it is our ideal. . . . It would point to the Parthenon as a good example of simplicity. It is both exceptionally simple, and richly loaded with content. Then I would point to Brunelleschi's Pazzi Chapel [Mordvinov: And to Le Corbusier on the Myasnitskaya?’]. ¹ And to Le Corbusier on the Myasnitskaya. I consider that a number of Le Corbusier's works are on a level with those ofBrunelleschi. A number of the Constructivist propositions on which we have been working remain true even now. . . . First and foremost, the organic nature of architecture. We set ourselves the task of discovering forms which derived from the function of the materials and the structure. This proposition remains valid, but it needs to be developed, since, at an early stage, although functions meant both utilitarian and ideogical functions to us, we nevertheless gave priority to utilitarian functions. The quest for a new socialist model which was to develop into the quest for a new image, also stands. Unfortunately, there also remains the struggle against eclecticism. . . . There remains the struggle for the incorporation of the latest achievements of building technology. . . . In the past, some of our comrades, though admittedly a minority of them, definitely deviated in matters of art. They claimed that art was a vestige of the past and promoted artistic labour in its place. Those comrades, however, who did not agree with them, myself included, did not fight against this deviation actively enough. But we never allowed the slogan “Away with Art" to appear in our journal. . . . Constructivism must constantly develop and move forward. . . .’ (Arkhitekturnaya gazeta, No.13 (85), 3March 1936) Melnikov (1890–1974) Konstantin Stepanovich Melnikov was born in Moscow, entered the General Educational Section of the College of Painting, Sculpture and Architecture and, on completing this course, continued his education, first in the College's Painting Section and then in that for Architecture, from which he graduated in 1917. In early Soviet years, Melnikov worked in the Moscow Soviet's architectural studio and contributed to the Architectural Department of the People's Commissariat for Education. In 1920–21, he was one of the organizers of an Architects' Section in the Union of Art Workers and became a member of the Section’s Presidium. He was a professor in the Architecture Faculty of Vkhutemas from 1921 to 1924. In 1922–23, Melnikov took part in three major architectural events of the early years of the decade, with entries for a Palace of Labour and a dwelling complex competition, and the Moscow exhibition's Makhorka pavilion. His design of the sarcophagus in the Lenin Mausoleum was executed in 1924, as was that of the Novo-Sukharevsky Market in the same year and that of the Soviet pavilion at the Paris International Exhibition in 1925. Melnikov was one of the acknowledged leaders of the new direction. He was active as a contributor to competitions and carried out numerous commissions. Five clubs, four garages and his own house were built from his designs. He produced an experimental design for garages in Paris in 1925; competition entries for the Leningradskaya Pravda building in Moscow in 1924; for the Columbus Monument in Santo Domingo in 1929; for Green City in 1930; for the Moscow Central Park of Culture and Leisure, the Moscow District Soviet of Trade Unions Theatre and the Palace of Nations (a counterproposal in the Palace of Labour competition) in 1931; for the Ministry of Heavy Industry in Moscow in 1934; and others. In Chapter 2/The leaders of the new direction 1931, Melnikov put forward a proposal for redesigning the Arbat Square in Moscow. His artistic position was close to that of the Rationalists, and he even joined Asnova for a while. As an architect with a markedly individual approach, however, he did not regard himself as belonging to any particular trend. In 1933–38, Melnikov headed one of theMoscow Soviet's architectural studios where he produced a number of designs, among them those for a Palace of Culture in Tashkent in 1933 and the development of the Kotelnicheskaya and Goncharnaya Embankments in Moscow in 1934. He was also teaching during this period at the Moscow Architectural Institute. Towards the end of the 1930s, Melnikov carried out a number of commissions and devoted much attention to painting. In subsequent years, Melnikov was mainly engaged in teaching, but also took part in some competitions, such as that for a Palace of Soviets in 1958 and a children's cinema on the Arbat in Moscow in1967. Melnikov's writings Extracts from an article entitled The Delineation of a Design’, 1933 ‘There is no obligatory sequence whatsoever for the creative processes applied in the initial stage of work on any design. Very much depends on intuition and what is still broadly known as “creative imagination". No work of any kind is possible, of course, on the conception of any structure without some preliminary study of the technical and economic features of the task in hand. But every now and again the spatial treatment and composition take shape in the architect's mind before the detailed work on the economic and technical considerations has started. . . . When oneis considering a design, ideas dart about in the constant search for a solution. Much has repeatedly to be reconsidered as diametrically opposite possibilities are rejected. Personally, I make no use of drawings and sketches of any kind in the course of my work. Not only have I no urge to do so, but I regard it as simply unnecessary, and even counterproductive. A drawing hampers the free play of ideas by promoting a ready-made treatment in graphic form, even though the mind may have rejected it. Drawings hamper me particularly at the initial stage of work on a design. All purely technical – as opposed to compositional – sketches are another matter: they are both useful and unavoidable in the architect's work. I have never had occasion to study any pattern books or manuals, or pay special attention to other people's constructions. The true architect has no need for this at all, even when he sets out to satisfy a particular stylistic canon. Nevertheless, since every architect hasstudied at a particular school, has been trained in particular tastes and has favourite masters, it is entirely natural that in the course of his creative activity he should, often quite unconsciously, be drawn to the classics or the influence of his contemporaries. It is appropriate at this point to examine two closely connected, but mutally incompatible concepts: the “related" and the “imitated". A master may be so familiar with the culture of former centuries that he quite unfailingly – one describes it as acting organically – becomes the bearer of the stylistic traditions of many generations. That is the quintessence of any culture, and it is therefore understandable that any master may see himself as a “relation" of some classical or even closely contemporary artist. An “imitator"is something else again. He merely copies mechanically and produces pale reflections, impotent tracings of what he has either never perceived or never studied. The most painful stage of the creative process is provided by the final touches to a design. How is one to picture to oneself the future structure in its natural environment, so as to decide whether it may not need some alterations or additions? However exquisitely a drawing is executed, it never satisfies the architect. A model provides an even more imperfect rendering of nature. That is why I regard it as obligatory that the architect should share in every stage of the construction from his design.... I should like to say a few words about the architect's collaborators . . . Though an architect's assistant may not act purely as a technical collaborator, his basic tasks will still be the elaboration of individualdetails, estimates etc. In fact, one sometimes wonders what to dowith oneself while most important compositional details are being elaborated – how one is to work hand in hand with an assistant . . . Even when an assistant improves upon individual compositional details in adesign, he may very often pervert the architect's basic idea. An assistant must have a particularly congenial nature, if he is to carry out joint creative work with an architect.' (Arkhitektura SSSR, 1933, No.5, p.35) Extracts from an article entitled The Assimilation of New Materials in Architecture’, 1934 It is not necessary to demonstrate that the latest building materials and structures influence the emergence of new styles of architectural construction. So much is obvious. . . . Ferro-concrete is a material which has been tried out in the most varied contextes. A caveat of a different sort must, however, be entered here: this building material has not as yet been fully mastered. I am convinced that it holds a multitude of possibilities and a great power of architectural expression, which our master builders still need to discover in this most flexible of building materials. . . . I am aware that an opinion is current among architects according to which we should, for all intents and purposes, have the leading and controlling part reserved to us in the production of building materials and structural components. I am not convinced that this opinion is correct. In the light of economic and technological circumstances, and bearing in mind the requirements of our building work, the construction industry itself must manufacture and market on a mass scale the requisite types of building materials and structural components. It is merely up to the architect to make skilled and artistically effective use of them." (Arkhitektura SSSR, 1934, No.4, p.37) Extract from the article on The First Place for Architecture’,1934 ‘That which is described as architecture is majestic. The loud language of granite in architecture arouses a millennial echo in the people's hearts. Architectural forms send forth a great clamour from one century to the next and preserve the freshness and glory of their times. The value of architecture lies in its bold creations.' (Stroitelstvo Moskvy, 1934, No.1, p.9) Extract from an article entitled The Creative Mood of the Architect’, 1934 I regard it as extremely important for an architect to be capable not only of designing in his studio, but of carrying out his design on the building site.' (Arkhitektura SSSR, 1934, No.9, p. 10) Extracts from an article on The Principles of Architectural Creativity’, 1936 Architectural creativity is an extremely complex process, a synthesis of art, science and technology, involving both the participation ofa large number of ancillaryworkers, and workers in construction, who implement the designer's conception and the quality and selection of building materials. . . . . As we see it, the retarding principle in architecture derives from the fact that certain architectural forms produced by historial evolution and subject to the laws of permanence, are so exquisite that they hold their own further development captive. . . . Only the genuine talent of a design artist endows his works with the right to live. Yet among us architects, the number of works of compilation – some of which are formally brilliant – has reached enormous proportions. As we see it, these works will soon be deprived of their value by life itself. . . . Only that architect will be worthy of our era who can encompass this great era of heroic struggle for the building of socialism and is willing and able to strive for genuine creativity. Given a talented architect, genuine creativity is destined to create a new era in the evolution of architectural art. It must provide humanity with monuments which will make it possible to appreciate the heroism of our time. It is essential to study the laws of proportion and composition embodied in Greek temples, and the entire architectural experience accumulated by humanity in the course of previous eras. But it would be as untoward to allow an architect whose output is characterless to approach actual building work, as it is to see a painter unskilled in drawing." (Raboty arkhitekturnykh masterskikh [The Output of Architectural Studios], Collected Articles, vol. 2, Studio No.7, pp.3–4) ## Leonidov (1902–59) Ivan Ilich Leonidov was born at the Vlasikh farmstead in Tver Province, as the son of a forester. He spent his childhood in the country; was apprenticedto an icon painter; entered, first, the Free Art Studios in Tver in 1920 and then the Moscow Vkhutemas in 1921, where he joined the Faculty of Painting, and later that of Architecture in Alexander Vesnin’s studio. Leonidov designed competition entries from 1925: for an improved peasant hut and dwellings atIvanovo-Voznesensk in 1925, and fora university in Minsk, as well as for model Workers' Clubs with 500 and 1,000 members, in 1926. While still a student, he joined the Constructivist organization, Osa, and contributed to their periodical SA. His 1927 diploma design for a Lenin Institute represents an important landmark in the evolution of Constructivism. After graduation, he remained at Vkhutemas as a postgraduate student, where he also worked as an assistant in Alexander Vesnin's studio. Soon afterwards, he headed a studio of his own in the Architectural Faculty. Leonidov's most active and fruitful years extended from 1927 to 1930. He was active in Osa, debated and produced experimental designs and competition entries, many of the latter on behalf of Osa, including a film factory in Moscow, a socially new type of club, a Government House in Alma-Ata and a Tsentrosoyuz building in Moscow, in 1928; a House of Industry in Moscow, in 1929–30; the Columbus Monument, in 1929; a socialist settlement for the Magnitogorsk Industrial Combine, and the Palace of Culture in Moscow, in 1930. At the end of 1930, Vopra set off a debate about Leonidovism', in the course of which his experimental designs were subjected to devastatingcriticism and he was forced to give up teaching. During the early 1930s, Leonidov worked in various design organizations on the planning and construction of the town of Igarka and on designs for Serpukhovo Gate Square in Moscow, for the reconstruction of Moscow, for the Pravda newspaper’s club etc. He produced one of his best works in the design for the People's Commissariat for Heavy Industry in Moscow, in 1934. During the second half of the 1930s, he worked on designs for the Klyuchiki dwelling complex near Nizhny Tagil, the Artek Pioneers’ Camp, the Usole settlement in the Urals, and on a number of interiors. In the post-war period, Leonidov overcame the creative crisis which had fettered his artistic imagination over several years, and sketched out a number of designs which bear witness to a renewed creative drive, including designs for a town of the future, Sun Town; a United Nations building; a Palace of Part I1I/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos Soviets; a Moscow World Exhibition; a Forum of Arts, and various others. Leonidov's writings A Note Concerning the Problem of the Monument’, 1929, about the Columbus Monument design Aim: The nations in their millions must be informed about the great man of action and his historic part in the development of modern culture. The monument must act as a condenser of all the achievements of worldwide progress, a site for the dissemination of information about the life and deeds of the man of action and the flow of world history. ## Means: This aim cannot be kept to a “monument"restricted in its means of logical impact and its small radius of action. The present day, with its unlimited scientific and technological progress, makes it possible to provide both for the aim and for the means of implementing it in universal terms. Radio and pictures carried by radio over a distance, an air and sea port condense the movement of universal culture. Radio, radio pictures, an air and sea port, the cinema, a museum etc, are means for informing the world about the life and role of the man of action and the achievements of universal progress. The museum is the centre of the monument,where all the memorabilia connected with Columbus’ life, as well ashis body brought from the “shrine", are kept. ## Construction: The large area of the museum is coveredby reinforced glass. Bearing in mind the nature of a tropical climate, and the purpose of the museum, a powerful jet of air is released in place of walls, thereby providing the requisite insulation. On hot days, an artificial stream of air also passes over the roof. The chapel is covered by a glass dome surrounded by a spiral ramp which enables one to look into it from every angle. The scientific laboratories represent the brain of the monument, where its plan of work is processed. We have: (1) the Radio Centre; (2) the Film Department; (3) the Observatory; (4) the Institute for Interplanetary Communications, the task of which is to solve the problems of interplanetary communications by means of the latest achievements of science and technology: the demonstration area for the latter is provided by the airfield and thesurrounding terrain, as well as by a part of the sea; (5) a hall for world scientific and technological congresses organized by means of radio.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1929, No.4, p. 148) ## Extracts from the lecture The Socially New Type of Club’ given at the First Congress of Osa, 1929 . . . The culturally educational work of the trade unions must provide an extensive service to meet the direct requirements and needs of the working masses, by creating cultural living conditions which ensure comprehensive educative development for the workers, and organizing cultural leisure and entertainment for them. In this connection, the cultural educational work of the trade unions must genuinely cover the widest masses of the working class, eliminate vestiges of apolitical attitudes and restrictive culture-mongering, reshape its method from the start and actually take first place in the whole work of the trade unions. . . .The trade unions must find their support among the most conscious, leading strata of working men and women and carry on active systematic work to develop the elements of the new way of life. . . .In order to involve strata of workers who are not so far being catered for, it is essential not to restrict cultural work within the framework of the clubs, but to develop it within the enterprises themselves, the workshops, workers' barracks and hostels, and workers' settlements, as well as setting up cultural educational work in the countryside in those places where industrial workers and seasonal workers are congregated, while closely linking this with the whole mass political and propagandist work of the Party.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1929, No.3, pp. 103, 108, 110) Extracts from Leonidov’s answers to questions put to him about his lecture devoted to A Socially New Type of Club’ at the First Osa Congress, 1929 Question:Will the worker rest in your club? Answer: The worker will rest in the club, at the sanatorium, the rest house and in his own dwelling. There is no such thing as absolute rest. Whatever a person does, tires him. But relative rest from one kind of work is possible by engaging in another (rest from “physical" work by engaging, for example, in "mental" work). A person's working day, cultural development and leisure can only be organized on the basis of the processes of labour. Question: How soon will economic and technological circumstances allow such clubs to be built? Answer: Today.Only sceptics and conservatives and lovers of tradition can ignore the scientific-technical facts available to us today, and fail to understand that with their help any cultural work desired can be organized. Those who do not see further than their own noses, who suggest that the cultural revolution should be carried out inthe old-fashioned way, that the land should be tilled with a wooden plough instead of a tractor and that the masses should be inoculated with enthusiasm for industrialization through “art", while industrialization is implemented in the handicraft way – only such would regard today's means of cultural organization as cloud-cuckoo-land. Question: Do you take into account the effect of light on organisms, and how is one to manage with your glass walls in Baku: how will people shield themselves there against strong sunlight? Answer: Climatic conditions must, of course, be reflected in the make-up of walls, and one cannot mindlessly transfer to Baku what has been done for Moscow. Question: How is one to account for the identical forms which you introduce for different functions, otherwise than by aesthetic formal considerations? Answer: The question indicates that the person putting it is primarily interested in outer form, in tasting rather than in organizing. Such a question is appropriate wherever there is concern for idealist architecture “as an art", while we are concerned with form as a corollary of the organization and functional interdependence of the workers and structural considerations. One should consider and criticze the methods of cultural organizations, not the form. Question: What is this – a piece of fiction or a design? Answer: That depends on the degree of understanding: there are those for whom Soviet power is not power, but a piece of fiction. Question: What size of site should be set aside for your club? Answer: The hygienic and operational considerations involved in the organization of a club require first and foremost a site well shielded from dirt and dust (a park, the outskirts of a city etc).It is thereforeclear that the more room, the better.The minimum area is two hectares. . . . Question: Do you take into account the effect of light on an individual's psychology? Answer: Light undoubtedly influences an individual's psychology and thewhole question is howto switch from the unconscious play of light to scientifically informed work with light. Question: How do you organize an individual’s emotions? Answer: Emotions and feelings are by no means abstractions incapable of scientific analysis, and the organization of emotions and feelings is primarily the organization of human consciousness. Question: Do you see any need at all to organize visual perceptions? Answer: It is not a matter of organizing visual perceptions, but of the general organization of human consciousness. The eye is an accurate mechanism which transmits the visual image to consciousness. The negative and positive nuances of assessment of visual perceptions depend upon the individual and social class experience of that consciousness.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1929, No.3, pp. 110–11) ## Extract from the explanatory note attached to the design for aPalace of Culture, 1930 (1) To impart a definite sense of purpose to all cultural work. To create the conditions for a hundred percent coverage of workers by polytechnic and political education. (2) To produce a clear sense of organization promoting the manifestation of initiative and self-help among those workers visiting the Palace. (3) To make the Palace of Culture not only a location for concentrated mass cultural work and leisure, but also the directing centre of cultural work in the entire working-class district. . . (5) To apply to the fulfilment of these socio-political tasks the most powerful resources of science and technology, and novel ways of conducting mass cultural work.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1930,No.5, p.4) Extract from the explanatory note attached to the entry for the House of Industry competition In our conditions, every new structure is a step towards socialism and must meet the new conditions of work and everyday Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos life. An architect who disregards these conditions is a conservative. Work is not a regrettable necessity, but a sense of purpose in life. Work as a physical and psychological condition must be totally organized. Indices of the new organization of buildings Organized work, work and physical training exercises, light, air, organized rest and food, heightened vitality. Indices of the old organization of buildings Confined courtyards, lack of vistas, pokey rooms, lack of adequate ventilation and light, barrack-like corridors. Absence of planning organization. Nervousness, piles, lowered vitality, lowered productivity.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1930, No.4, p. 1) ## Extracts from the explanatory note attached to the design for a socialist settlement at the Magnitogorsk Industrial Combine, 1930 A socialist settlement is not the old random accumulation of urban districts and barracks, divorced from nature, haphazardly tied to industry, robbing people of their vitality by its dullness... A socialist settlement is a carefully thought-out organization of industry and agriculture, culture and leisure – of everything that informs human consciousness and life. A settlement constructed on the basis of the foremost socialist technology. ... A habitat should be conceived not as a kind of masonry holdall, with noisy corridors, where thousands of people dwell deprived of light and individual leisure, divorced from nature, but as a means of organizing small communities in which one personality is not lost among a thousand others but, instead, has the opportunity to develop to the utmost and to communicate with people (proceeding from a small community to larger ones). Habitats surrounded by gardens, sports grounds and swimming pools which dispense with the need to arrange rest homes outside the city. Habitats in which work, leisure and culture are organically linked.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1930, No.3, p. 1) Extract from the explanatory note attached to the competition entry for a design of the People’s Commissariat for Heavy Industry (Narkomtyazhprom), 1934 Until now the Kremlin and the Church of St Basil have served as the architectural centre of Moscow. Naturally, the erection of a new, grandiose building on Red Square will in its turn influence the part played by individual monuments in the general effect of this central complex of Moscow. I consider that the architecture of the Kremlin and St Basil must be subordinated to the architecture of the House of the Narkomtyazhprom and that the NKTP building should itself occupy the central position in the city. The architecture of Red Square and the Kremlin is delicate and majestic music. The introduction into this symphony of an instrument of vast size and powerful sound is only permissible if such an instrument takes the lead and surpasses all the other buildings in the ensemble by its architectural quality. Neither pomp nor overblown tawdriness in details and forms should provide the basis of the NKTP House composition, but, instead, simplicity, strictness, harmonious dynamism and wealth of meaning. The historical patterns must be subordinated for purposes of composition to this outstanding object, in accordance with the principle of artistic contrast.' (Arkhitektura SSSR, 1934, No. 10, p. 14) Extracts from an article entitled The Architect’s Palette’, 1934 The technology of building materials and structural elements is one of the decisive factors – togetherwith the social conditions of everyday life – which determines the style and general composition of an architectural construction. Everybody knows that is was our Functionalists who first formulated this statement with full clarity. But it is appropriate to point in this connection to a cardinal sin committed by contemporary archietects, both in the USSR and in the West. Their approach to the latest building materials has been purely mechanical. They started exclusively from a primitive view of the everyday functional purpose of a structure, without turning creatively to the new organization of humanity in terms of function and way of life, and this led to a certain utilitarianism in their design solutions. The artistic – and even, to some extent, technological – potential of the latest building materials has not been fully understood. . . . Architects make an extremely ponderous use of such a marvellously flexible and expressive building material as ferro-concrete.... The architect should not approach building technology from a narrowly structural point of view. He must familiarize himself in a philosophical sense – if one may use such an expression in this context – with the potentialities of building technology. He must create new forms and structures from the material concerned. This is an elementary creative need which requires definite boldness in the search for new forms. But this will also in itself suggest new means of resolving the spatial and general stylistic tasks facing the architect. . . . The production of building materials and structural elements must involve the close co-operation of the architect. He must act as a consultant or even control this branch of our industry... . It is essential to clarify the question of standardized production of individual structural elements and large building components. Some designers also have in mind the mass production of large “artistic" building components. This is a typically eclectic approach to architectural problems. Plastics will undoubtedly come to occupy in future a place of honour among our latest building materials. Plastics open unlimited opportunities for the widespread and skilful use of new materials and one can boldly assert that they will play a part just as revolutionary as that of ferro-concrete at an earlier time. . . . The colour treatment of façades, including, of course, those built of concrete – either by colour rendering or some other form of protective material – is obligatory. The colour, however, should not disguise the building material by overlaying it, but should stress its features, so that they may achieve the greatest possible effect.’ (Arkhitektura SSSR, 1934, No.4, pp.:32–33) Extracts from a speech given at the All-Moscow Conference of Architects, February 1936 I have been tagged with a number of “isms” – I am a Constructivist, and a Formalist, and a Schematist, and so on. In fact, I have been a Constructivist all along, and have worked in the Constructivist Group. . . . Thereis enormous confusion concerning creative method. An architect's working method is primarily the method of an artist, and there is no standard for the method of an artist. To- Chapter 2/The leaders of the new direction day I achieved a splendid ground plan, and tomorrow Iwill achieve a splendid elevation to go with this plan, or the other way about. . . . I do everything in my power to make things real and concrete. . One should believe in socialism – and it is hardly a fault to dream a little in this connection. (Arkhitekturnaya gazeta, No. 13 (85), 3 March 1936) ## Lissitzky (1890–1941) Lazar Markovich Lissitsky was one of the most diversely gifted artists of the twentieth century, who made major contributions to architecture, painting, theatre design, photomontage, industrial design, book production and exhibition design. He studied at the Architectural Faculty of the Higher Technical School in Darmstadt, 1909–14, and at the Riga Polytechnical Institute, 1915–18; was one of the founders of Unovis; taught at the Free State Art Studios, SGKhM, 1919–20; and was a member of the Moscow Inkhuk in 1921. Lissitzky was instrumental in the incorporation into architecture of the achievements of Leftist painters – principally the Suprematists – with his series of Proun paintings and drawings in 1919–24. He lived in Germany and underwent medical treatment in Switzerland during 1921–25, but maintained his artistic links with the USSR and promoted Soviet art – in particular, Constructivist work. He was in close contact with a number of European architects and artists, was a member of the Dutch De Stijl group, a co-founder of the periodical Veshch/Objet/Gegenstand, jointly with Ilya Ehrenburg, in Berlin, 1922, as well as of ABC, jointly with Mart Stam and Hans Schmidt, in Zürich,1924, and he represented Asnova abroad. During the early Soviet years, Agitational Art played an important part in Lissitzky's output, well-known examples being the poster Cleave the Whites with the Red Wedge of 1919, and the Lenin Tribune of 1920–24. In 1923–24, he worked on problems of the vertical zoning of a city area in his designs for horizontal skyscrapers’ in Moscow. After returning from abroad, Lissitzky was active, both creatively – with designs for a yacht club in 1923 and the House of In- Part I1I/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos dustry in 1930, among others – and editorially, on behalf of Asnova – with the publication of one issue of Izvestiya Asnova (Asnova Neæs) in 1926, together with Ladovsky. He took part in architectural competitions, with designs for the House ofTextiles in Moscow, 1925, dwelling complexes in Ivanovo-Voznesensk, 1926, and the Pravda newspaper complex in Moscow, 1930. He taught the design of multi-purpose furniture and built-in equipment for standard dwelling units at Vkhutemas-Vkhutein in 1925–30, and introduced a number of radical innovations in exhibition design at Soviet pavilions abroad, in Cologne in 1928, and Leipzig and Dresden in 1930, at the All-Union Polygraphic Exhibition in Moscow in 1927, and others. He also contributed to the interior spatial organization of theatres producing, for examples, the design for I Want a Child at the Meyerhold Theatre in 1928–29. He perfected a method of visual and spatial book design with Skaz o dvukh kvadratakh (The Tale of Two Squares), 1922, Dlya golosa (For the Voice), 1923, and Khorosho! (Good!), 1927, by Mayakovsky, in the periodical SSSR na stroike (USSR on the Building Site), 1932–40, and other publications. Lissitzky's writings ## Extracts from theses for the Prouns (From Painting to Architecture’) 1920² Not universal illusions, but universal reality 1. Proun is what we called the station on the way to the construction of a new form. . . . From being a copyist, the artist becomes a constructor of the forms of a new world of objects. This world is not built in competition with the engineer. The paths of art have not yet crossed the paths of science. 2. A Proun is a creative structuring of form (proceeding from mastery over space) with the aid of an economic construction of the material used. The role of the Proun is a staged motion along the path of concrete creativity, and not the demonstration, explanation or popularization of life. The Proun's path does not lead through the narrow and fragmented individual scientific disciplines – the constructor concentrates allthese within his experimental experience. The Proun's path is not at all the inconsequential path of individual scientific disciplines, theories and systems, but the clear path of influence acknowledged – of reality. . . . 4. We have investigated the first stages of our construction, confined to two dimensions, and found that it is just as solid and resistant as the Earth. One may construct here as one would do in three-dimensional space and the tension of forces between individual parts must therefore equally be brought into balance. The conjunction of the resultants of individual forces manifests itself in a novel way in the Proun. We have seen that the plane of the Proun ceases to be a painting and becomes a structure which one must contemplate in the round from every side, examine from above and investigate from below. It follows that the only axis of the painting perpendicular to the horizon has been destroyed. As we circle around, we spiral inwards into space. Until now, we projected space directly on to the plane. Through the Proun, we are put in the position of having to effect our escape from this plane of projection. We have set the Proun in motion and have thereby acquired a multiplicity of axes of projection; we stand among them and push them asunder. As we stand within this framework, so we must begin to define it. .. . 10. The Proun is virile, dynamically active. The Proun moves from one stage to the next, along a line, towards perfection. The Proun leads to the genesis of the future, it attracts a new plane, then switches to the modelling of space and beyond this to the construction of all forms of life in the widest sense of the word. The Proun alters the accustomed forms of art and leaves the picture to the individualist, a scarcely perceived phenomenon, who sitting in front of his easel in a closed studio starts and finishes it alone. The future life is the ferro-concrete foundation of communism for all the peoples of the world. With the aid of the Proun, the integrated city commune in which all humanity is to dwell will be erected on this foundation. And so the Proun passes on beyond the painting and its painter on one side, the machine and the engineer on the other, and strides out armed with new elements for the creation of a new space, articulates it by means of elements of 1, 2 and 3 dimensions and constructs a new, multifarious, but integrated image of our nature.' Extracts from an article entitled The Catastrophe of Architecture’, 1921 We now stand on the road leading the artist to direct participation in the creation of a material culture. Architecture has always been an art which imparted material substance to the culture of an era. What is afoot in our architecture ...? Ever since our living, natural, creative activity has been stuffed into academic hothouses, all that was truly creative has bypassed these musty boxes. Yet those who found canvas and clay a constraint and went on to create structures, found themselves captives in the incantatory ring of an infinite number of bookishteachings, with which the solidly established professors crammed them and, slowly but surely, bottled up the whole creative urge of their apprentices. And thus monsters grew up among us, walking encyclopedias of all bygone centuries.... But then fresh winds began to blow in the West. . . . . . a new image of the new bourgeois, capitalist world came into being via the image ofits city. When imperialist capital began to tread on the corns of its younger brother – the petit bourgeois – the taste for nature and philanthropy was born. In London,Ebenezer Howard invented the “garden city”, small, comfortable houses on the outskirts where those for whom there was no room at the centre might seek their salvation. The German Krupps and their peers seized upon this splendid idea, and began to philanthropize their workers with such colonies. Such are the worlds whence the wind wafted us our living corpses, who are now preparing to force on communist Russia the capitalist imperialist city, with its stock exchange at its centre (the City) and, swept awayto the fringes from where no breeze blows, its manufacturing back alleys, its philanthropic little houses for the “working" classes, and so forth and so on. They are now trying to devise a standard single-family worker's house. . . . But do we need this? Do we need a separate house of our own... . . . would labouring Russia be content with this? .. . It now turns out, however, that our venerable ones are engaged in the art of architecture, while all the rest is just the art of building. Let the builders build, let them put up the living body of a factory or a popular dwelling and the artist-architects Chapter 2/The leaders of the new direction will then come along with the powder and make-up and wigs and beards appropriate to style, in order to plaster and powder the builder's inartistic product. . . . Is not architecture in these circumstances a parasite on the sound body of a structure? . . . And yet life is crying out for creators. Where can we expect them to come from in the art of construction, that field which faces the tremendous task of reconstructing the entire body of lifein accordance with the new content that is being created for it.' (Gazeta Izo’, 1921, No.1) Extracts from the periodical Merz, 1924 It is more than ENOUGH, this constant MACHINE MACHINE MACHINE MACHINE as soon as one comes to modern art. The machine is no more than a brush, and a very primitive one at that, with which the canvas of the world's picture is being painted. . . . It would be a total waste of time, to put it mildly, to try to prove nowadays that, given the existence of the typewriter, it is no longer necessaryto write in one's own blood with a goose quill. Trying to prove nowadays that the task of any creativity, including art, is not representation, but affirmation, is also a total waste of time. The machine has not divorced us from nature. We have discovered bymeans of ita new, previously unsuspected nature. Modern art has reached the same conclusions as modern science by entirely intuitive and independent means. . . . And in so doing both have reached the same formula: EVERY FORM IS THE PETRIFIED SNAPSHOT OF A PROCESS. THEREFORE, A WORK IS A STATION IN EVOLUTION AND NOT ITS PETRIFIED AIM. We acknowledge works which embrace a system, but a system that has become manifest in the course of production, not before it. . . . Our work is no philosophy, nor a system of natural science, it is a branch of nature and only as such can it become an object of cognition.' (Merz, Hanover, April/July 1924, No.8/9) Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos Extract from a letter to J.J.P. Oud, 1925 I am convinced that art arises wherever we least anticipate it. I go to the utmost pains to think out and make use of every aspect of the functions, constraints, material possibilities etc, but I only experience satisfaction if a work results which surprises me when I am confronted by it. I believe that all my organs taken together are more correctly on course than my brain is on its own. If I lacked this conviction, I would rather become a cobbler tomorrow." Extracts from an article entitled Americanism and European Architecture’, 1925 The words “America” and“American” are linked in the Old World, in Europe, with the notion of something extra-perfect, rational, efficient and universal. All these are concepts alien to the old artists of Europe. . . . Our era has invented a new material – concrete. A house can be poured from it like a statue in bronze. It has some drawbacks, however, for housing purposes. At present, work is in hand everywhere on the development of new materials that will be light, easily processed, thermally insulting etc. And, in this, Europe is beginning to outstrip America. It is in the solution to this problem that the centre of gravity of work devoted to the creation of a new form also resides. . . . Europe takes over organizational and practical ideas from America, then purifies and refines them. In this, Europe sets itself the target of meeting more than the demands of economy, efficiency and hygiene. European architects are convinced that by designing and organizing the house afresh they will be sharing in the process of organizing a new, more conscious way of life. We have had occasions to meet in Europe a number of excellent masters of the new architecture and discover how difficult their situation is. They are surrounded by a chauvinist, reactionary, individualistic society which regards as alien and hostile these internationally minded, cheerfully active and collectively inclined people. That is why they follow with such attention the progress of our life, and all believe that the future belongs, not to the USA, but to the USSR. (Krasnaya niva [The Red Cornfield], 1925, No.49, pp. 1188–89) ## Extract from the article The Film of El’s Life up to 1926 . . . Proun. The picture canvas has become too narrow for me. The circle of colour-harmony epicures has become too narrow for me, and I created the Proun as a transfer station from painting to architecture. I have treated thecanvasand the wood panel as sites on which my structural ideas would meet with the smallest number ofobstacles laid across their path. I have used the black-and-white scale (with red highlights)as substance and material. In this way, a reality will be created which will hold no ambiguity for anyone.' (El Lissitzky. Maler, Architekt, Typograf, Fotograf. Erinnerungen, Briefe, Schriften. Übergeben von Sophie Lissitzky-Kuppers [El Lissitzky. Painter, Architect, Typographer, Photographer. Reminiscences, Letters, Writings. Related by Sophie Lissitzky-Kuppers], Dresden, 1976, p. 329) ## Extracts from an article entitled The Culture of the Habitat’, 1926 Salons, halls,boudoirs, drawing rooms. . . all have been swept away and all that is left is bare living space. But this niggardly, cramped living space is already beginning to grow into the new Soviet habitat. Forms of dwelling are the material expression of essentials of the new way of life, and they demand our attention. Let it be understood that the habitat, its plan and its organization, represent the basic cell out of which houses are formed – houses which in their turn form streets and squares, in other words the city as a whole. This is growth in the direction of“large architecture". But “small architecture" also develops from the organization of the habitat, its interior equipment, its furniture. . . . the present time does not merely demand, it cries out for a new type of lodging, for new equipment, for new furniture. The fundamentals of our way of life are as follows: equalization of the requirements of all layers of the population; a standard amount of living space; and the new Soviet family. Equalization of requirements is the starting point in the elaboration of a standard dwelling, as opposed to bourgeois countries where total anarchy prevails in respect of requirements. The standard and the definite limits demand inventiveness directed at rationalization, planning and equipment. A modern lodging must be designed like the very best modern suitcase, taking into account all the necessities which must find a place in it, and making use of every cubic centimetre– all else apart, this is dictated for the present by the need for economy. The new Soviet family, with factually equal rights for husband and wife, accords the wife far more rights than merely those of nurse and cook, and imposes more obligations on her, which, in turn, set new requirements in terms of habitat. . . . . . . Our basic task is to plan the habitat so that the bulk of equipment and furniture are built-in together with the house, as a single entity . . . All the kitchen equipment . . . the cupboards, partitions, folding tables, beds– all this can quite simply be built as part of interior space. Such a taskrequires the joint efforts of an architect anda designer of domestic equipment.' (Stroitelnaya promyshlennost [Building Industry], 1926, No.11, pp.877–81) ## Extracts from the Asnova slogans, 1926 Our grandmothers believed that the Earth was the centre of the universe and that Man was the measure of all things. . . . Learn to see what is in front of your eyes. Man is the tailor’s measure and architecture must be measured by architecture.' (Izvestiya Asnova, Moscow, 1926, p.8) Extracts from an article on The Architecture of the Steel and Ferro-Concrete Framework’, 1926 Functionalism, Constructivism, Horizontalism and Verticalism . . . such are the slogans round which architectural thought revolves today. Construction by means of new materials – steel and ferro-concrete – has resulted in horizontal and vertical articulation of the skeleton of a house. This skeleton has evolved from the new requirements set for a modern house, from its novel functions. . . . The plan and its system, that is what determines the total outcome. Let us take note of the most characteristic feature of a modern plan, which is the proportion between black and white; the proportion between the total sectional area of walls, supports and partitions and the area of living space. The more modern the plan, the smaller this fraction will be. It is thus that we come right to the heart of the new method of construction.... And so, the unchallenged master of modernity – the engineer – has created the steel and ferro-concrete framework. Architecture, the master craft that is atpresent still lagging be- Chapter 2/The leaders of the new direction hind modernity, must take onsuch construction. Nothing comes into the world of its own accord, and architecture will not result from a single structure. That would necessitate a whole series of other, no lessimportant conditions. Our architecture must bring forth its own masters. Experience creates masters. Experience creates actual building work.... Here we still regard as novelty designs on paper. The steel and ferro-concrete framework predominates in them, but it is a graphic rather than a material framework, and therefore practice alonewill succeed in providing us with genuine criteria,.. . We are faced with the task of creating spatial architecture which is not only seen by the eye from a distance, as in painting, and not only touched by the hands, as in sculpture, but among which people live and move – an architectureof space and time. To this end, steel and ferro-concrete frames may provide us with excellent tools.' (Stroitelnaya promyshlennost, 1926, No. 1, pp.59–63) ## Ilya Golosov (1883–1945) IlyaAlexandrovich Golosov was born in Moscow as the son of a priest. After leaving school, he entered the Moscow Stroganov Industrial Art College and then, in 1907, the Moscow College of Painting, Sculpture and Architecture, from whichhe graduated in 1912 as an architect. As a student and immediately after graduation, he worked as an assistant for a number of architects and artists, surveyed architectural monuments and was engaged in book production. Private houses in Kirzhach and Moscow were built to his designs in 1912 and 1913 respectively. During the First World War, he built various structures for use by the army's rearguard forces. During the first years after the Revolution, he worked in the architectural studio of the Moscow Soviet and taught both at the Moscow Polytechnical Institute andat Vkhutemas. In 1918–19, he produced à numberof designs in the Classical mode, such as a hospital and crematorium in Moscow, and a Leo Tolstoy Memorial School at Yasnaya Polyana. In the early 1920s, Golosov adopted Symbolist Romanticism, developed a novel theory concerning the construction of architectural organisms and introduced a new method of tuition in the Vkhute- Part HHI/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos mas studio which he jointly directed with Melnikov. The most characteristic of his experimental designs and competition entries produced in 1920–24 included those for a bakery, a radio station, an observatory, a workshopwith water pump; for the Ostankino stud, the Palace of Labour in Moscow, the Far Eastern pavilion at the 1923 Exhibition and for the Soviet pavilion at the Paris Exhibition, as well as for the Arkos and Leningradskaya Pravda buildings. In the mid-1920s, Golosov switched to Constructivism and went on to produce, until the early 1930s, a large number of outline sketches and competition designs – many of which won prizes and were carried out – for buildings and complexes of various kinds. Among these were office buildings, such as the House of Textiles in 1925, the Elektrobank and Rusgertorg buildings in 1926, the House of Military Industry Trusts in 1929 and the House of Industry in Sverdlovsk in 1931; Palaces of Labour like that for Rostov-on-Don in 1925; Workers’ Clubs and Houses of Culture, for Moscow in 1927–29 and Stalingrad in 1928; communal houses and dwelling combines for Moscow in 1927–28, Ivanovo-Voznesensk in 1929–32 and Stalingrad in 1930, Houses of Soviets for Bryansk in 1924, Khabarovsk in 1928–30, Rostov-on-Don in 1930 and Elista in 1928–32; and mass action theatres, notably for Ivanovo-Voznesensk in l931, Sverdlovsk in 1932 and Moscow in 1933. From 1933, Golosov headed one of the Moscow Soviet's architectural studios. During the first half of the 1930s, he took part in debates about the creative orientation of Soviet architecture, andcriticized both Constructivism and the direct application of Classicism.
