He made increasing use of traditional forms in his designs dating from the 1930s, such as those for: the Palace of Soviets in 1932; dwelling houses in Moscow on Yauza Boulevard in 1933–35 and Kalyaevskaya Street in 1936; the Tass building in 1934; the House of the Book in 1934; the Moscow Trade Union High School in 1938; and a dwelling house in Gorky in 1936. During the Second World War, Golosov designed a large number of memorial structures, such as graves, mausoleums, pantheons and arches. During the last years of his life, he worked hard on a major book, Osnovy sovremennoi arkhitekturnoi kompozitsii (Foundations of Modern Architectural Composition), which he never completed. Ilya Golosov's writings Extracts from manuscripts dating from the early 1920s Architecture, like every science, must have firm principles of its own. . . . It abounds with laws concealed from us until now. Common sense demands that these laws be elicited and obeyed.' Architectural form cannot be achieved through knowledge alone. Sensitivity and artistic intuition must also be present. Yet artistic intuition develops through knowledge. Intuition is a capital that is accumulated gradually, but can be spent at one go. 'When composing a group of structures, or a single structure, it is essential to distinguish mass from form. . . . we will assume the former term to cover any volume of the most rudimentary kind, devoid of any inner meaning; in other words, not resulting from any particular subjective architectural idea. An architectural mass as such is without content and is not the expression of a particular idea – it is therefore totally free in the forms it adopts and bears no responsibility for them. We shall contemplate every architectural structure in terms of both mass and form, without confusing these two concepts. When considering an architectural structure from the point of view of mass, we must wholly put aside its conception, thereby freeing ourselves from the responsibility of impinging on its subjective form, and approach it from a purely objective angle, without any direct reference to its individual conception. In other words, the “architectural mass” is the most rudimentary three-dimensional form, carrying no subjective inner architectural meaning whatsoever and quite independent from such a meaning. . . . If mass carries no inner meaning, bears no responsibility towards the latter and is wholly independent, the opposite is true of form: it is dependent upon and responsible to the meaning which has brought it into existence. When we perceive a form, we experience the inner meaning. There can be no architectural form without a definite meaning, since if a form lacks meaning it must simply be described as an architectural mass and, conversely, if an architectural mass is invested with a definite meaning, it ceases to be a mass and becomes a form. . . . Consequently, any architectural structure can be regarded as a mass defining its position in space and a form (or a complex of them) bearing the imprint of a particular functional character derived from its inner meaning. Mass is a phenomenon the chief property of which is power, weightiness ... it can be contemplated independently of form.... Form is the expression of essence – the meaning of a particular object. It marks out the limit of a phenomenon. Architectural form is the expression of architectural thought.' When one analyses architectural mass and form, one cannot help giving one of the highest priorities in terms of perception to the principle of movement. In every mass or form a correlation of forces expressing this principle is always present in one shape or another. . . . One of the main conditions governing meaningful composition in architecture is an understanding of the properties of the configurations of masses or forms employed, in relation to their repose or movement, and inherent in them in accordance with their nature. . . . Every structure as a whole has a dominant direction of movement of its own. . . . it is essential to postulate in this context that movement must be understood, not as a change of location, but . . . as the conjunction of movement with a static scale, i.e. a structure which embodies clearly expressed movement is nevertheless seen as strictly static.' Themajority of monumental buildings must incorporate movement and the concept of total repose can only be applied in veró rare cases – it is permissible only in those cases when a single structure brings together a very large number of buildings and is sited apart and entirely independently.' ‘Achieving that harmony among masses which must be present in every structure means achieving harmony among all movements.' There is no limit to the number of ways in which a cube can be used– each ofits surfaces can provide the basis for the whole form, A prism is less adaptable in terms of position. An eightsided prism cannot adopt a prone position, because its base would lack an area of adequate size. A cylinder is similar in this to an eight-sided prism. A pyramid is dependent on the number of its faces.' Life – the world rotates faster and faster. . . . Repose in antiquity – movement in modern times. The need to subordinate architecture to a clearly marked Chapter 2/The leaders of the new direction rhythm becomes ever more obvious and the brilliance with which it is expressed indicates the worth of a piece of architecture. In terms of expression, architecture has perceptibly shifted in recent years from detailed forms to general masses. Our era is gradually destroying an earlier concern for details and history. Life requires construction, the artistic creation of that which is complete and individual – mainly the construction of interrelated masses. The value of this very kind of construction abolishes nowadays any more thorough study of a structure's details as a starting point for architectural composition. The rhythm of masses – that is where compositional innovation in architecture finds its start.' When composing any group of separate structures linked by a common purpose, the architect is at liberty, within the limits of a rational treatment, to choose any particular general composition to cover all the structures.’ (Tsgali [Central State Archive for Literature and Art], Fond 1979, Schedule I) Extracts from a notebook entitled Ideas, Statements, Aphorisms, 1920s The creative drive, crushed by the majesty and beauty of Classicism, can create nothing new. ‘General architectural composition devoid of principle cannot be redeemed by any embellishments, by any exquisite details.' The modern reproductions of Classical architectural models resemble an adaptation of Procrustus’ bed to the human frame.' Plastic quality is among the basic requirements of an architectural building.' Repetition is fundamental in art.’ ‘Size (scale) can suppress worth.' The confusion, mystique and vagueness of the theory about a golden mean in architecture are features which forbid one to accept it unquestioningly.On the contrary, everything indicates that such a theory fetters living creative forces.' Nature abhors a vacuum. In architecture, as in all manifestations of life, gaps, empty spaces ask to be filled: an architect must therefore learnto . . . see these gaps so as to fill them." (Ilya Golosov archive) Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos Extracts from a lecture entitled The New Ways to Archtecture’, Decémber 1922 ... The architect must be free of style in the old, historical meaning of this term, and must himself create a style resulting from an architectural form that matches the meaning or purpose (idea) of the object. Guiding rules and laws must be provided for this purpose so as to make it easier in each particular case for the architect to pick the right path towards a solution of the given artistically creative task. . . . . . . It is essential to lay down only constant principles, that are irrefutable, true and immutable. There are very many such principles, and these principles, which have an absolute intrinsic value, are certainly equally valid for Classical architecture and for the architecture of our time. All the rest is a matter of intuitive creativity and cannot be confined to the hard-and-fast framework of law. . . . The first and immutable law of architectural artistic construction is the law of artistic necessity. Other extremely useful laws are those which concern spatial positions and the interrelations of architectural masses; the proportional artistic relation of volumes; artistic perception; the expression of the psychological effect of artistic-architectural masses and forms; the independence of certain phenomena; and many other laws. There are certain given natural effects over which the artist has no control whatsoever, since he is only in a position to make a certain use of their actuality, such as, for example, the effect of horizontal and vertical, light and shade etc. There are principles which can only be assessed in objective artistic terms. Every work of art must manifest the artist's individuality, but such manifestation will only be justified and artistically meaningful ifit expresses an objective value, since an unknown quantity can only be determined by means of a known quantity. This obliges us to adopt laws with an absolute value that constantly and immutably underpin every work of art.' (Iz istorii sovetskoi arkhitektury 1917–1925 gg. Materialy i dokumenty, Moscow, 1963, p. 29) Extracts from an article entitled The Large Architectural Form’, 1933 .. . Architecture is one of the most complex forms of art. . . . When I set to work on a design, I tackle the volumes, the elevation and the plan jointly, rather than mechanically distinguishing between them. Starting from a general conception of the task, I move to the composition of a plan. Every architect must give thought to the connection between the volumetric solution and the handling of the ground plan, while taking account of the functional purpose of the structure and its general social and aesthetic conception. I always start on a design by making a close study of the technological and economic particulars of the task. This very often even happens at the expense of a certain “impressive" quality in the design, which, on the other hand, becomes considerably more realistic. Generally speaking, the constant revision of individual parts of the design is inevitable. And this is where the preliminary sketch or drawing plays a colossal part. The drawing gives concrete expression to the individual stages of an architect's work and informs him of the correctness or otherwise of the treatment applied. A drawing is especially necessary and profitable at the initial stage of design work. . .. To an architect, the ability to draw matches the ability to assemble. An architect who is not skilled in drawing will never be able to compose.' (Arkhitektura SSSR, 1933, No.5, p.34) 1 First Moscow Architects’ Studio (Work Guild), 1919. ## Ginzburg (1892–1946) Moisei Yakovlevich Ginzburg was born in Minsk as the son of an architect. He graduated from the Milan Academy of Arts in 1914 and the Riga Polytechnical Institute in 1917; carried out his design for a private house in Evpatoriya and investigated Tatar national architecture in 1917–21; taught at Vkhutemas from 1921 and became professor of Architectural History and of the Theory of Architectural Composition there from 1923. Simultaneously, he taught at the Moscow Institute of Civil Engineers – Migi, later MVTU – and was confirmed as a full member of the Russian Academy of Artistic Sciences – Rakhn. During the first half of the 1920s, he worked on problems of architectural composition, as in his book Ritmv arkhitekture ![](images/250e23eee1eaa74ed15ef4638cd2c345d9682b5581511e75edb9d2bfe9c02033.jpg) Senior master builder: Zholtovsky (20). From left to right, standing: Savinov, Pasternak; seated: Afanasev, Ginzburg Barshch, Nikolai Sokolov. ![](images/73b32589df01c8ec48e9f3549f8356c91f5a3df9a9fa17d9761b539333eb5e44.jpg) ![](images/9209bbcd6c5592ff8d3d3a15b17dc13915ab34a414d620a3254ebac8763b153e.jpg) ![](images/cc3e40e8814d33e7dd36a9594a0ab9c39ddafe2a5135bb63c1f04521c3991948.jpg) ![](images/59fca626b6a8012e3353d69285860d67db177eaabec92ec812a2a055c0778bd3.jpg) ![](images/4530cafc0fa2c357347ea2738e6ee6eb8a560d3334ff26b74bbd0174f698074c.jpg) ![](images/b9fba008d143fc020d03269c6cfcf2c0ac76a2d1d844b0525e47c2df5f7763cc.jpg) ![](images/d29672a7055280d0758e2369eae501c15ca34303449ecde52466dd63f9ccf2e1.jpg) (Rhythm in Architecture) of 1923. He was active in the struggle for the new architecture in editorial articles for the periodical Arkhitektura in 1923 and in his book Stil i epokha (Style and Epoch) in 1924. He was one of the organizers of Osa in 1925 and joint editor of their periodical Sovremennaya arkhitektura (Contemporary Architecture) – SA – in 1926–30. During 1926–27, he published in SA a series of articles on theory in which he set out and promoted the fundamental positions of the Functional Method – the creative statement of Constructivist tenets. During the years 1928–32, Ginzburg headed a team of architects whose aim was to study the problem of a scientific organization of the way of life and of the standardization of housing. The team produced designs for new types of economic, rationally planned dwelling units and houses provided with communal services.Ginzburg later summarized his conclusions from this work in his book Zhilishche (Habitat), published in 1934. During the 1930s, Ginzburg went deeply into problems of socialist settlement and district planning, producing designs such as that for Green City in 1930, the planning of the Chernikov industrial district in 1931and the regional planning of the southern shore of the Crimea in 1935–37. Throughout the twenties and thirties, Ginzburg produced a large number of competition entries, including the Palace of Labour in 1922–23, the House of Textiles in 1925 and the Orgametal House in 1926, in Moscow; the House of Soviets in Makhachkala in 1926; a Housing Commune in 1927 and the Government House in Alma-Ata in 1927–31, which was carried out; a theatre in Sverdlovsk in 1931; the Palace of Soviets in 1932, the Nemirovich-Danchenko Theatre in 1933, the Narkomtyazhprom building in 1934 and the Izvestiyacombine in 1936, all in Moscow. An apartment house was built in Moscow in1926–27, andthree experimental communal houses of a transitional type were erected in Moscow and Sverdlovsk in 1928–29 to designs by Ginzburg. In 1935–37, Ginzburg headed the team which constructed a sanatorium complex in Kislovodsk. Towards the end of the 1930s, Ginzburg was in charge of work on a multi-volume General History of Architecture at the USSR Academy of Architecture. During the war, he dealt with the standardization and industrialization of building work. Towards the end of his life, he started on the reconstruction of Sebastopol and two sanatoriums, at Kislovodsk and Oreanda in the Crimea, which were not completed until after his death. His major theoretical work dealing with architectural creativity also remained unfinished. ## Ginzburg's writings ## Extracts from Ritm v arkhitekture (Moscow, 1923) The history of styles asconceived until quite recently was merely a history of the evolution of architectural form. The compositional methods which went into the construction of actual monuments were left in the background. Yet, here too, the characteristics of these laws of composition must be elicited if a style is to be fully understood.’ (pp. 71–72) ‘The history of styles, like every other history, is objective. It does not recognize the existence of“better” or “worse”styles. A style ultimately works itself to death. . . . A new influx of creative power is needed in order to start afresh. . . . Yet what is dead will not rise again. Identical problems endlessly gain in complexity, the methods for their solution change. . . . And the task of modern architecture is, of course, to discover those formal elements and those laws governing their combination through which the rhythmic beat of our time will be revealed.' (p. 116) ## Extract from an article entitled The Old and the New’, 1923 We can distinguish two driving principles whenever a new style makes its appearance: the law of continuity, which reinforces our artistic invention and imagination, while economizing our creative ability and energy, and the law of independence of the new idea, which finds its support in the new life that emerges around the artist and which imparts to the work the edge of novelty and that feeling for rhythm of modern times without which art simply ceases to be art.' (Arkhitektura, 1923, Nos. 3–5, p. 3) ## Extract from an article entitled The Aesthetics of the Present’, 1923 If before the war and the Revolution we could still hope for success in searching out all kinds of Renaissances and Classicisms, the last few years have, of course, made us clearly aware of the obvious truth that going forward is easier if one also looks ahead. However half-baked, confused and far-fetched architectural ideas among the young may be, they nevertheless relentlessly point to the fact that some very marked change has taken Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos place in the nature of human emotion and its attendant aesthetics, a change which, whether it be good or bad, is certainly reflected in the still inchoate explorations of young art.' (Arkhitektura, 1923, Nos. 1–2, pp.3–4) ## Extracts from Stil i epokha (Moscow, 1924) Experience, backed by the creativity of past centuries, clearly points the way for modern artists: boldness in experimentation, daring in the quest for novelty, joy in creative discovery; that long thorny path that leads to victory, provided only that sincerity is in the movement, and determination shines brightly, and that both are carried to the shore by a wave that does not falter and meets the needs of our time. That was the way of art in all the best phases of humanity's existence and that, of course, is what it should be today.' (p. 12) . . . the system of an architectural style arises in answer to a whole number of problems – those of space and those of volume, which represent the treatment of the same task from inside and outside.' (p.23) In addition to the assessment of a workof art or an entire style in terms of history, i.e. in its relation to the environment which brought it into being, another method of objective assessment arises in genetic terms, aimed at establishing the significance of a phenomenon for the further development of that style and the evolution of the process as a whole.’ (p.24) An excellent indication of the creative impotence of our times is provided by the very notion of making new city districts fit in with the style of old existing ones, however formally excellent these might be. This is a notion which has established itself very firmly in the minds of the best architects of the last decade and has often forced them to subordinate entire city quarters and districts to the formal features of some group of stylistically earlier monuments, instead of subordinating them to the organic reality of that city, something that lies entirely beyond the formal features of any particular style.' (p.27) Almost invariably, a new culture is brought into being by the appearance on the stage of history of a new nation, ethnicity or social group. . . . Certain forms of artistic organism are most representative of each period of history or, more accurately, of every active creative drive. . . . The component of life which the new active social element of modern times – the working class – has promoted to first place has been labour because it represents the main ingredient of life for this element, its unifying feature. . . . Thus the main problems which come to the fore today concern those architectural organisms which involve the concept of labour – the workers’ house and the house of work – and the multiplicity of problems they pose. And the logical conclusion to be drawn from this in the light of a study of history, is that the significance of these problems will outgrow their initial solution and will provide the basic elements of a new generally applicable style.' (pp. 77–80) The problem of the workers’ house was posed by life long before the war. . . . Even at these early stages, we encounter the spirit of collectivism, a sweeping increase in architectural scale which favours lapidary and energetic statements. We have no concrete idea of what the workers' house of the future will be like, but one can foretell without much fear of being proved wrong that it is precisely these qualities, derived from the characteristics of the workers’ dwelling as such, which will provide its essentials.’ (pp.80 and 82) Quite apart from a speculative perception of the laws of statics and mechanics, every individual has a purely intuitive understanding of these laws. . . . Thus, on the strength of our perceptual experience and psychological features, the constructive system also generates another system, self-contained but derived from and dependent upon constructions in the world of form – strictly speaking, a system of aesthetics." (pp. 112 and 114) When a new stylistic idiom makes its appearance and its new elements are formulated,there is clearly no need for extraneous accretions – whatever is new most often arises from a constructive or practical requirement without any decorative addition. Later, decorative elements gradually appear, at first without disrupting the organic life of the monument concerned, until their abundance oversteps these limits and turns into a self-involved interplay of decorative elements. The youth of a new style is mainly constructive, its maturity is organic and its decay decorative.’ (pp. 119 and 120) . .. if during the last century, when life meant something different, the main architectural objects were the dwelling and the public building which exerted an influence on industrial architecture, a diametrically opposite phenomenon is now taking place: industrial architecture, which is more closely related to the sources of modern perception of form, must exert its influence on the architecture of dwellings, which is dominated to a greater extent by tradition and inertia.’ (p. 134) Dealing with everyday matters in close conjunction with the foreman and the constructor, an architect cannot help catching theír methods of working. Like them, his aim will be a clear-cut solution of a task embodying particular data and particular unknowns, rather than the unbridled fantasy of an immaterial conception. The architect will then come to regard himself not as a decorator, but as an organizer of life. The spiritual energy and creative imagination thus released will not, however, desert his work but will merely be directed into another channel, with imagination turning into inventiveness. . . . In this way, subconscious, impulsive creativity will come to be replaced by a clear and distinct organizational method.’ (pp. 141–42) ## Extracts from an article entitled New Methods of Architectural Thought’, 1926 Instead of the old system of architectural work, in which the ground plan, construction and outer aspect of a building were constantly at war with each other and the architect was, in so far as he was able, the arbiter of all these unresolved conflicts, the foremost feature of new architectural creativity is its single, undivided, integrated drive forward, in the course of which the task is evolved organically and which represents the creative process from start to finish. . . . The quality of a modern architect's work is radically altered by the fact that he does not regard his activity as the fulfilment of individual commissions, butas a matter of establishing architectural standards governing the production of new dwellings and cities, and of constantly perfecting these standards. . . . As socialism is currently being built up, every new solution provided by the architect –for a dwelling, a club, a factory – is perceived by us as the invention of an entirely new model, appropriate to its task and suitable for reproduction in whatever numbers the State may require. This deflects the architect's energy at the outset from hankeringafter individual and idiosyncratic treatment, and directs it towards the improvement of his standard and the refinement and maximum standardization of all his details. . . . The new architect, free from all vestiges of the past, prejudices and bias, analyses every aspect of his task and its special features, reduces its component elements, groups these by function and organizes his solution in accordance with these factors. ... As a result, we observe in the output of modern architects the emergence of an entirely new, largely asymmetrical kind of plan. .. . . . . Only functional architectural thinking rigorously fixes spatial organization at the start of work. . . . It is from this that the modern architect principally proceeds. It forces him to develop his idea outwards from within, and not the other way about, as was the practice in periods of eclecticism.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, No.1, pp. 1–3) ## Extract from an article on The International Front of Modern Architecture’, 1926 ‘Modern Soviet architecture, at least such of it as is grouped around our periodical . . . does not contain any nihilism whatsoever and does not, under any circumstances, reject the requirements of formal expressiveness. It is, however, entirely based upon the functional aspects of a building as a whole, and of its constituent elements. Our line on modern architecture is based on the principle that a finished work of architecture, just as any other genuinely modern object, is not a building or an object comprising some sort of aesthetic additive, but a concrete task, organized in a rational and planned way, the very method of organization of which provides its maximum potential of expression.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1925, No.2, p.44) Extracts from an article on The Functional Method and Form’, 1926 The Functional Method, correctly understood, requires from the modern architect, in every case without exception, a material presentation arising from the new conditions of life. If an atavistic form ultimately results from the architect's work, the Functional Method must have been incorrectly applied and the solution is faulty in some respect. . . . It should be most categorically stated that the Functional approach does not . . . abrogate the highly important task of architectural presentation. It merely establishes the laws of such presentation, and forces the architect to look for it in those elements that are functionally justified, by removing him from Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos the realm ofunrelated aesthetic additionsand derivation, to the organization of the purely architectural task self and the formal use of allthe practical and structural opportunities which are always available within any architectural project.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1926, No.4, pp.89 and 91) ## Extract from an article on The National Architecture of the Peoples of the USSR’, 1926 The Functional Method, correctly applied, should also provide an answer to the question of a national image of architecture. All the conditions determining the contemporary image of the National Soviet Republics must be taken into account: (1) the conditions determining the character of the secular way of life and climate, a character which defines the individual national image of a republic; (2) the conditions determining the new social structure, the new ways of life . . . and the achievements of modern technology, all of which are common to and alike in the USSR as a whole, and define the growth of the Union-wide forces of socialism under construction. A correct solution can only be arrived at by taking both categories of conditions into account. From this point of view, of course, the revival of old architectural forms of decoration appropriate to one or another of the national styles cannot be justified in any way.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1926, Nos.5–6, pp. 113–14) Extracts from an article entitled Purpose in Modern Architecture’, 1927 Functional architecture could not exist without a clearly and newly stated purpose. This purpose, which is often only conveyed by a single word – factory, club, dwelling etc – must first be carefully analysed by the architect, and then given substance and broken down into a system of clear production and domestic processes. Production or labour processes are usually associated in our minds with a factory or industrial plant, while socially domestic ones are connected with dwellings and public buildings. . . . in fact, during the construction of socialism, socially domestic processes must, of course, be treated with as much care and attention as production processes. (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1927, No.1, pp.4–5) Extract from an article entitled Constructivism as a Method of Laboratory and Educational Work’, 1927 Any study of the elements of architecture involveș a danger that certain forms may be canonized and pass into the architect's vocabulary for everyday use. Constructivism wages war against this phenomenonand treats the basic elements of architecture as material that is constantly changing in accordance with the changing conditions of the generation of form.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1927, No.6, p. 165) ## Extracts from a lecture entitled Constructivism in Architecture’, 1928 The theory of Constructivism may be subdivided into three parts: the Constructivist attitude to the aim, to the means and to the form. . . . the aim, in so far as we are concerned, is the absolute forceful demolition of old concepts. This is usually a programmed process within which the architect must display his creative ability. . . . Our aim is not, in fact, the execution of a commission, as such, but working together with the proletariat on the tasks involved in the building of a new existence, of a new way of life. . . . technology is for us no more than a means which we press into service. . . . All the achievements of modern science must be subordinated to our work, must be tied in to this work for a new social aim by the new architect. . . . A fairly widely held opinion is current that Constructivism represents artistic nihilism and is the rejection of form, an unwillingness to take account of form. In reality, however, Constructivism is a working method for searching out the most reliable and correct path to a new form, which most closely corresponds to the new social content. . . . we approach form through the unfolding of the social aim. Form, in so far as we are concerned, must constantly be sought and newly defined each time by an aim that is clear and bears the imprint of the Revolution.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1928, No.5, p. 145) in 1912. He went to Italy for a year after graduation to study architectural monuments, then taught at the Institute and, as an assistant to the architects Vasily Kosyakov and Alexei Bubyr, helped to design and erect various buildings in Petersburg, Reval (now Talinn) and elsewhere. During the first years following the Revolution, Nikolsky was engaged in the design and building of civilian structures on the railways. In 1920, he resumed his teaching work at the Institute ofCivil Engineers, and also engaged in practical work. At the start of the 1920s, Nikolsky was influenced by Cubo-Futurism and Zhivskulptarkh, as in his design for a District Executive Committee building in 1921. After 1926, Nikolsky went on teaching, but also took charge of an architectural design studio, whose members formed somethinglike aLeningrad branch of Osa. A whole series of experimental and competition designs were produced here, such as a tram stop, a hall for public meetings, a club, a crematorium, a cinema with dining hall etc, all of which may be classified as examples of Suprematist Constructivism. During the 1920s, Nikolsky was much concerned with the development of models for socially new types of structures. In the housing field he did a Housing Commune. Amongst communal facilities he did baths and a swimming pool; in the field of public buildings he designed clubs, schools, higher educational establishments and sports complexes. Amongst his projects actually executed in Leningrad were baths in Lesnoe and in Ushakov Street, schools in Lesnoe and inStachek Street, dwellings on Traktor Street, clubs for the Krasny Putilovets factory and the shipbuilding wharfs, and stadia for the Krasny Sportintern and Krasny Putilovets. Nikolsky took part in a large number of competitions, among them that for the Central Telegraph Office in Moscow in 1925, a communal house for workers – an Osa competition – in 1927,the Tsentrosoyuz building and the Lenin Library in Moscow in 1928, a public library in Leningrad in 1928 and the first round of the Palace of Soviets in 1931. ## Nikolsky (1884–1953) Alexander Sergeevich Nikolsky was born in Saratov, the son of a doctor. He completed his secondary-modern education in 1902 in Petersburg and entered the Petersburg Institute of Civil Engineers, from the Architectural Section of which he graduated His main work during the 1930s was a stadium for 100,000 spectators at the point of KrestovskyIsland in Leningrad, as part of a large Park of Culture and Leisure complex. During the war, he worked on the camouflage of a number of military targets in Leningrad, prepared a set of drawings of that city at war and designed a number of memorials such as monuments and arches. Chapter 2/The leaders of the new direction During the 1930s and the post-war years, Nikolsky was very active in the Union of Soviet Architects. ## Nikolsky's writings ## Extract from a lecture on the new construction of schools, given at the First Conference of Osa, 1928 As one of the most important factors in the country's economic life, the construction of schools and cultural educational establishments, including clubs, adult education and pre-school institutions, is very closely conditioned by the present ecónomic situation and the availability of funds. Together, these will determine the direction which building for cultural purposes is to take in the immediate future. It must be carried out in the most closely planned and rigidly economical fashion. In this connection, and in view of the limited resources at present allocated by the State for the needs of national education, the rationalization of a model school building and a design calculated to bring it up to modern requirements acquire special significance, starting from the planning and construction of the building itself and ending with the provision of specialequipment.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura,1928, No.4, p.114) ## Extracts from an article on Natural Lighting for Interiors’, 1929 ‘The question of the natural lighting of interior premises in a multi-storey building, except for industrial purposes, had until now raised no questions and was resolved by the only means possible, with the openings situated in the lateral outer walls. If the nature of the premises required much light, the window space was increased, and vice-versa. As to the distribution of the apertures admitting light, this has followed the set pattern inherited from the brick wall . . . I have posed the question of a rational distribution of lighting areas to suit the needs of one form of inner space or another. The attempt to secure an equal amount of light by means of a smaller, more rationally distributed area of lighting takes the problem somewhat further, since it involves not only rationality, but economy in the use of natural light. . . . work on the study and rationalization of natural lighting should not be restricted to the multi-storey structurę with its obligatory lighting through lateral outer walls. Part HII/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos On the assumption that the ideal method of illumination of interior space is overhead lighting, a critical study of this possibility must be made in connection with the use of multi-storeybuildings.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1929, No.2, p.57) ## Extracts from an article About the Works from My Studio’, 1934 Factors in architectural work such as building materials, structural elements, questions of cost, engineering and technological considerations, public health requirements and functional domestic installations have been subjected to some study and their treatment has, one way and another, been put on a scientific foundation. Architecture as a spatial art, on the other hand, in terms of form and proportions, as an art uniting all its component parts in a harmonious whole, has not yet become a subject for scientific study. . . . A theory of architecture must be created, an architectual grammar must be formulated. Everybody needs such a grammarbook as a matter of basic information. Yet for us as architects, this is not a case of knowledge but intuition. A systematic grammar is the key to a reading of the past and the future. . . . . . . The social and economic circumstances in which we live provide all the prerequisites for the development of a new architectural style. . . . : . . I would like to dwell . . . on the study of the problem of natural lighting of premises and the changes in the exterior aspect of a building connected with this. This work revealed to me what architectural innovations, what unexpected and varied possibilities, what a broadening of the creative range can open before an architect who is free and liberates himselffrom the grip of traditions of every kind.' (Akademiya arkhitektury [Academy of Architecture], 1934, Nos.1–2, pp. 92–93) ## Extracts from an article entitledCreative Report’, 1935 We are experiencing years of historical transition in the evolution of our architectural thought and practice. . . . On looking back now one might, of course, claim that the positions taken up were not ideal, the standard of equipment was poor, there was no wholesale co-ordination between the operations of individual units, thatinternal frictions and quarrels even occurred and that, as a result, the losses were too high. But it is bevond question that the aim and the plan of campaign were correct, that the tactics, generally speaking, met the needs of the situation and that the moral spirit of fighters and commanders was beyond reproach. That is why, as I see it, these honourable positions and glorious battlefieldsshould be respected, the size and significance of the losses should not be exaggerated, and what has been conquered should be favourably assessed. In short, the architectural trends of the post-Revolutionary era were quite predictably conditioned by economic, social and technological changes. The struggle of“Leftist architecture"against epigonist apeing, eclecticism and the uncritical adoption of Classical models from West and East was equally predictable and correct. What was, however, wholly incorrect was the indiscriminate rejection of art in general, together with the architecture of the past, as being ideologically hostile and technologically outdated forms of art. . . . A certain reaction against vulgarization, joyless schematicism and other youthful sins is psychologically understandable. But when as a result of such reaction we are presented with a series of designs and buildings that are overloaded, excessively pompous, eclectic, pseudo-monumental and pseudodecorative, a serious architect can only be moved to regret, even if the “beauteousness" of these buildings continues to impress the public at large for some time to come.’ (Arkhitektura SSSR, 1935, No.4, p.52) ## Extracts from notes written at various times A modern artist may not take an “heir" unto himself. The modern architect must bring forth his own children, not take them on temporary lease. Thus, for instance, a House of Culture must grow out of the demands of modern life, and not out of the rejigging of a gambling club and an Imperial Theatre.' (1933) The architect must be capable of finding his way without difficulty in all social, political, economic and technical questions of the present time. He must fully take into account territorial, climatic, ethnographic and other conditions in each individual case. The architect must make use of all the opportunities offered to him by modern science and technology.' (1933) Economy is the first word inscribed on the architect's banner. Economy is the direct meaning of the word – economy in the means of expression etc.' As a doctor who examines, auscultates and investigates a patient in an attempt to discover any weak spots, entirely so as to strengthen what is debilitated, cure what is sick and remove whatever is incurable, so I – having received a theme – check the organism of a building for soundness: I look to see whether there are not some sick or ailing places, and whether some requirement may not be dictated by decadent, and therefore unhealthy, traditions. In a word, I take the task in hand and improve it to the best of my ability. Life calls upon the architect to take a creative and active part in its reshaping, to assist in the birth of new types of buildings. It drives architecture forward, in hot pursuit of technology, science and social ideas which have all forged ahead of it.' (Quoted by A. A. Ol in his essay The Creative Views of A. S. Nikolsky', in Tvorcheskie problemy sovetskoi arkhitektury [Creative Problems of Soviet Architecture], Leningrad, 1956, pp.85–106) ## Viktor Vesnin (1882–1950) Viktor Alexandrovich Vesnin was born in Yurevets and graduated from the Moscow Practical Academy, and in 1912 from the Petersburg Institute of Civil Engineers. He worked on a whole series of architectural designs during the years 1908–14, in conjunction with his brothers Alexander and Leonid. During the First World War and the early years after the Revolution, he worked mainly on his own and specialized increasingly in industrial architecture, with designs for chemical factories in Kineshma and Tambov Province in 1915, the Zhilevsky chemical factory near Moscow in 1916, the building complex of the Great Kineshma Tomna factory in 1916–17, the superphosphate factory at Chernorechensk in Nizhny-Novgorod Province in 1918–19, the sulphuric acid factory in Saratov in 1920–22, and the rosin and turpentine factory in Vakhtan in 1922–24. Features characteristic ofRational Architecture were most prominent in these works by Viktor Vesnin, and this trend not only influenced some of the designs for civil buildings undertaken jointly by the three brothers before the Revolution, but represented one of the sources of Constructivism. Chapter 2/The leaders of the new direction Viktor Vesnin began to teach at the Architectural Section of the Moscow Higher Technical Institute – MVTU – in 1923, where, together with Alexander Kuznetsov, he decisively influenced the formation of a Soviet school of industrial architecture. Vesnin worked in close collaboration with his brothers in 1923–24, but also produced a number of individual designs during this period, among which the most interesting of those actually built were the Institute of Mineral Raw Materials in Moscow in 1925–28, the Agricultural Bank in Ivanovo-Voznesensk in 1926-27 and the Dneproges power station in 1929–30, in collaboration with Nikolai Kolli, Georgy Orlov and Sergei Andrievsky. In the 1930s and 1940s, Viktor Vesnin took a leading part in founding the Union of Soviet Architects and furthering architectural science. He also came to the fore as a prominent social and official personality. From 1932 onwards, he was chairman of the Organizing Committee, and then chief secretary, of the Union of Soviet Architects, as well as one of the organizers – and the first president in 1938–49 – of the Academy of Architecture of the USSR. ## Viktor Vesnin’s writings ## Extract from a speech made at the discussion of the competition entries for Dneproges, 1929 I belong to that trend and that group which considers it impossible to draw a line between architectural and engineering elements in a structure. In this case, form is no more than a byproduct, and if we work on the form at all, it is only in the sense of clarifying and perfecting it in processing the data which provides us with the basic, functional solution. . . .’ (Quoted by A. G.Chinyakov, in Bratya Vesniny [The Vesnin Brothers], Moscow, 1970, p. 124) ## Concerning the Functional Method The widely used old method of phased and fragmented design, dealing first with the plan, then the vertical section and, finally, the elevation and perspective, is basically unsound.' The connection between all the elements of a design is indissoluble, and any alteration of one of these invariably involves the alteration of another. . . . Every work of architecture is three-dimensional and one must therefore train oneself to design by working on the plan, sections, elevations and perspective simultaneously, in parallel and multilaterally. One must teach oneself to think not about separate projections and parts, but globally, about the structure as a whole.' (Chinyakov, op. cit. p. 90) Extract from an article The Language of the Epoch’, 1932 ‘The language of Classical forms, however perfect the latter may be, is the language of the past and cannot be used to express the present.' (Sovetskoe iskusstvo [Soviet Art], 15 March 1932) From a speech made at a discussion on art in May 1934 Too rapid a transition was made in architecture from the slogan “Impermissible" to “Everything is allowed". The transition from ultra-rationalism to ultra-non-rationalism has been too sharp, and yet, as I see it, the majority of architects have gone down this path of unbounded Decorativism. I describe this as . . ."Shchusism", and it is, in my opinion, a very dangerous phenomenon, maybe even more dangerous than Retrospectivism... It is said that richness is needed in architecture. Yes, richness is indeed necessary. But what we are being offered is not a richness of our own. It is the richness of the merchant's taste. Why do we find this richness alien? It is richness without rigour, not built on a hard foundation. It is a nauseating richness, the richness of make-believe.' (Arkhitektura SSSR, 1934, No.6, p.6) ## Krinsky (1890–1971) Vladimir Fedorovich Krinsky was born in Ryazan. After completing his secondary education in Petersburg he studied first at the Art School of the Society for the Promotion of the Arts, then at the Petersburg Academy of Arts, initially as a painter and later as an architect, in 1910–17. In 1918, Krinsky worked in the Directorate for the Restoration of Yaroslavl. In 1919, he joined Zholtovsky's architectural studio at the People's Commissariat for Education and became a member of Sinskulptarkh-Zhivskulptarkh at the same time. He joined the Working Group for Objective Analysis at the end of 1920 and the Working Group of Architects of Inkhuk early in 1921. He headed the United Leftist Studios – Obmas – in Vkhutemas, jointly with Ladovsky and Dokuchaev. After the restructuring of Vkhutemas in 1923 and thecreationof the Basic Section – the Introductory Course – Krinsky was in charge of the general teaching ofSpace' as a subject. Krinsky, together with Lamtsov and Turkus, summarized his experiences in teaching this discipline in a manual – Elementy arkhitekturno-prostranstvennoi kompozitsii (Elements of Architectural-Spatial Composition), published in 1934. His teaching work extended over more than half a century, until 1971, bridging the successive transformations of Vkhutemas into Vkhutein, Vasi and the Moscow Architectural Institute. During the early Soviet years, Krinsky produced a series of competition entries and experimental designs which reflected Symbolist Romanticist tendencies: a crematorium, a kiosk, a temple of the new cult, a Temple of Communion Between Nations, a communal house and a speaker's platform. During the 1920s and early 1930s, Krinsky, apart from teaching, also turned out a large number of designs, some in collaboration with Rukhlyadev, such as those for the skyscraper of the Supreme Soviet for the National Economy – Vesenkha-in 1922–23; the Arkos building in Moscow, the Soviet pavilion in Paris, the Lenin House of the People in Ivanovo-Voznesensk and the House of Soviets in Bryansk, all in 1924; the Mausoleum to Lenin and the Heroes of the Revolution in Odessa in 1925; a sports arena in 1928; the Columbus Monument in Santo Domingo in 1929; the Palace of Arts, as a member of the Asnova team, in 1930; and the Palace of Soviets in 1931, both of the latter in Moscow. During the 1930s, in line with the general orientation of Soviet architecture at that period, Krinsky made great use of Classical architectural forms in his designs, such as that for the Komsomolskaya Metro Station, with Rukhlyadev, and the locks on the Moscow-Volga Canal. As one of the leaders of Rationalism in the 1920s, Krinsky made an important contribution to the formulation of the theoretical tenets of this trend at the time ofits genesis in the Inkhuk WorkingGroup of Architects. Krinsky’s writings Extracts from the minutes of the Inkhuk Working Group for Objective Analysis: a discussion of The Analysis of the Concepts of Construction and Composition, and the Distinguishing Factor Between Them’, January−April1921 ‘As distinct from the architect, the engineer only uses material in its unprocessed form and only sets himself a utilitarian task when he devises a structure.' A Greek temple is a stone construction and modern architecture perpetrates a lie when it uses the proportions of a stone construction. Such architecture disfigures and destroys the essence of the material.' Construction – in the sense of the configuration of a building – incorporates material. I regard a well-defined construction as one that involves the configuration as a whole, while the combination and arrangement of supports represents composition. The conjunction and direction of forces, on the other hand, represents construction.' ‘There can be no merging of technical, i.e. utilitarian, with artistic construction, in the sense of any theoretical subordination, or of an integration of the latter into the former. A variety of aims is obvious. The main principle of construction in making both abstract and utilitarian things is economy. The constructive principle can be traced in composition as the essence of design. In painting, composition lies along the path to liberation from representation and turns to construction for the basic answer: that is why it is so difficult to separate composition from construction in painting (and sculpture). In architecture, construction is inevitably present as a technical and mechanical basis – the very basis of composition.' Construction implies a movement of force, or a direction, which is schematically expressed by lines. A system of lines is in itself a bare construction and every system of planes or spatial forms, when each of these is represented in motion, is a constructive configuration. A system of configuration involves the law of interaction between the elements of a construction. Architectural construction relies upon physical laws of stress and balance which, within a structure, determine the interaction of that construction's components with each other. (Inkhuk archive) Extract from a lecture to the Inkhuk Working Group of Architects on The Path of Architecture’, May 1921 Since art differs from nature by the fact that it is of “man”, what could be more human in man than thought . . . As to architecture, there is no need to demonstrate that perception of it is impossible without understanding the logic ofits treatment. What is basic in architecture, as in creativity and in perception, is the architectural idea. . . . There is no need to demonstrate that the perception of a particular structure is inseparable from an awareness ofits technical efficiency and of its appropriateness and reality at that moment. A disturbance of the harmony between the perception of it as a pure form and the perception ofit as a dwelling, when the latter is absent, makes for unreality and leads to a purely aesthetic perception which is no more than superficial or, as the saying goes, formal. We cannot fully perceive the architectural concept as an answer in this case, because one of the essential conditions of this answer is missing. . . . And so architecture always has a living theme. Living architecture consists in creating the present: And the architect has one weapon in this creative act – form. Form is usually described as utilitarian if its purpose is to fulfil a given practical purpose. It is said that the construction engineer's concept is material and that he resorts to a utilitarian form, as opposed to the architect, whose ideals are a matter of art and who employs artistic form. . . . We cannot conceive of a utilitarian form which would allow no latitude for its treatment as a form. In the majority of cases, an infinity of means is available for altering the given, wholly utilitarian treatment one way or another in terms of form. In other words, everyutilitarian treatment is also a treatment of form. It could hardly be otherwise, since we impart form to everything that we do. Man finds his way about in the “external" world owing to form. It would be impossible to create anything without formulating it, because we could otherwise not understand it. Our understanding of form depends upon some ofits particular qualities. . . . Line, plane and geometrical surface – such as a sphere, a cylinder or a cone – are eternal. They represent the laws of our thought in perception. The means of finding one's direction in the world consist of an order of perception Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos which we establish. That is why we say that there is no purely utilitarian form and will naturally talk only about real form. .. Is any preconceived idea, determined in advance, in fact, possible as part of the search for an answer, before the conditions of the task are known and, consequently, before the theme has been stated? All these aesthetic axioms and preconceptions enterińg into modern architecture are generally abstract and unreal, since they exist outside real tasks, so it is natural that they should dealwith abstract, extra-utilitarian form in general. This form is defined as having only one purpose – its impact on a human being. We have seen that real form also has an impact, since we would otherwise not perceive it. But abstract form has nothing besides its impact.We also know, however, that, just as art, its impact is achieved solely by its “meaning", i.e. in terms of thought and content. Meaningless beauty is air, sun, whatever animals may perceive. Art is human. What then is resolved by an abstract form? It indubitably has only one aim: the problem of the perception of form in architecture, sculpture and painting comes down to a perception of space. The aim of abstract form is purely theoretical. It is the theory of form in generalized terms. The scientific discipline is to think in space. One cannot apply abstract form to reality, or utilize it. A cube, a cylinder or a cone are not forms, but concepts by means of which we resolve a given form. This is not a predetermined idea. It is our way of speculating about real space. Yet every abstract form resolved represents a formulation of our experience of space. And we are thus forced to recognize that there can be no predetermined idea before a set architectural task has been resolved and its conditions have been studied. It may happen that an abstract form appears to be impaired by practical application, but this only goes to show that the task has not been solved correctly. An answer obviously results from the conditions, and conditions cannot be made to fit the answer. And to debate what leads the architect to select an answer, whether it is art, i. e. form, or material considerations, i. e. the utilitarian purpose, is like arguing about what came first, the chicken or the egg. Imparting form to real life – that is the architect's sole aim. (Inkhuk archive) Extract from Krinsky’s Credo’, December 1921³ Art escapes from the blind alley of aestheticism as from those of humanism and romanticism, by ceasing to be an end in itself. Form is an invention, not a cult. Its value is proved in action. The abandonment of aestheticism, even if it involves a total rejection of art, is already a sign of health; but pure utilitarianism is no way out either; with it, darkness sets in again, and the concept of bare efficiency is equally meaningless. I leave aside, as far as possible, everything that is not connected with architecture, assuming that the latter includes the whole cycle of spatial problems connected with the organization of the space that surrounds us. This is, of course, factually indistinguishable from what we call building work, from architecture as a technique. The architecture which was and is cultivated in academies, and is labelled as art, is also lodged in the blind alley of aestheticism. If we try to establish what leads to this dead end, we shall encounter the main stream of academic training and building practice, both of which originate from the Renaissance. We must assert that the Renaissance style, as it has been allowed to develop over the years through inertia, is a brake and an enemy to architecture, in so far as it represents an active factor within it. The Renaissance style can be no more than decoration, and is at best mere romanticism. The roots of architecture are lost in its case. As manifestations of architecture, the Classical and Renaissance styles are fetishes. It seems to many people that by destroying these fetishes we also destroy the very essence of architecture, and deprive it of all they value most. And one often runs across the honest query: "But what, then, is left?" What is casual and temporary has become fundamental, what is basic has been lost. Beauty as a cult of times past, as a kind of eternal value, is a fog that must be dispersed for good, and only by dispersing it will we emerge from our dead end, and our field of action will become enormous. We must inevitably be a bit barbarian: the work of fine adjustment lies before future generations, while now is the time for basic questions. We are left with much to do, our tasks grow immeasurably and life itself dictates them to us. Dwellings, schools, factories, railway stations, power stations, gardens, villages,streets, squares and cities.... WHAT factories, WHAT cities? One cannot retreat from this into the past. The other basic question which architecture still harbours is – HOW is all this to be done? In this respect, we proceed from the basic laws of our perception, from the operation of our eyesight, from our methods of understanding, and find our way about the space that surrounds us. . .. A straight line and a plane; further, the regular arrangement of planes and lines – a volume; further, systems of sequential arrangements of particular distances – rhythm. In parallel with this, come the essentials of material configuration – theoretical mechanics and construction. Questions ofform must be worked out architecturally. Form cannot be excluded from technology. Mastering technology without mastering form would mean always remaining a slave to tradition. Pure utilitarianism cannot be established as architecture's guiding thought: that would amount to as much of a fiction as pure art. An assertion that questions of form can be shrugged off during the resolution of utilitarian tasks would imply that the existing form was accepted as axiomatic, i.e that one was in the grip of the identical old aestheticism. The theory of abstract form is for consideration as a discipline of spatial thinking and points to the way of solving real problems. Chapter 2/The leaders of the new direction Abstract solutions of form are self-sufficient, but in terms of life as a whole this is no more than laboratory activity for the formulation of our spatial impressions. In the last resort, the sole material of architectural composition is our spatial experience." (Inkhuk archive) ## Architectural associations of the new direction ![](images/e1eab017e89473306eceb0e19ae2a8ec663f3c2c2d9346dec031d3e078a88dd0.jpg) ## Working Group of Architects in Inkhuk Set up in March 1921. Members of the group were Ladovsky, Alexander Efimov, Krinsky, Alexander Petrov, Dokuchaev and Mapu. Extracts from the Programme of the Working Group of Architects’, 1921 (1) The Working Group of Architects is engaged in the analysis and synthesis of the elements of architecture as means of expression. (2) The Group considers the chief, basic elements of architecture to be: space, form and construction. (3) Its secondary elements and means of expression are recognized as: mass, weight, volume, colour, proportions, movement and rhythm. (4) Since the essence of architectural solutions comes down to an ordered sequence of spatial quantities, the problem of the space used by architects as their material is by far the most important ofits basic problems, and is therefore subject to investigation by the Working Group first and foremost. (5) Subsequent tasks will be: form and construction; all the remaining elements of architecture will follow later. . . . (8) The psychology of perception to which the means of architectural expression ultimately appeal cannot be overlooked in the Group's research work. (9) The Group does not lay down as permanent any one method of research, on the assumption that – given the fundamental proposition that the means of architectural expression are, in essence, means of affecting individual perception – all possible methods and their combined use will be equally applicable and necessary for the investigation of the means of architecture.' (Inkhuk archive) ## Asnova: Association of New Architects Asnova gradually came together in the course of the development of Zhivskulptarkh (1919–20), Obmas (1920–23) and the Working Group of Architects in Inkhuk (1921–23). Its constitution was registered on 23 July 1923 and its founders were Ladovsky, Dokuchaev, Krinsky, Rukhlyadev, Alexander Efimov, Fidman, Mochalov and Balikhin, together withthe engineers Loleit and Kuzmin. The leader of Asnova was Ladovsky. Other members included Bolbashevsky, Borisovsky, Budo, Bunin, Bykova, Varentsov, Volodko, Gelfeld, Zalesskava, Korzhev, Kruglova, Krutikov, Vitaly Lavrov, Lamtsov, Lissitzkv, Melnikov, Viktor Petrov, Valentin Popov, Prokhorova, Silchenkov, Spassky, Travin, Turkus and others. The first and only issue of Izvestíya Asnova, edited by Lissitzky and Ladovsky, appeared in 1926. Ladovsky left Asnova with a group of his followers in 1928, when Balikhin became its leader. It became a sector of Movano in May 1930 and its Programmatic-Ideological Position’ was published in 1931. Asnova was dissolved in 1932, along with all other groupings. ## Extracts from theConstitution of Asnova’, 1923 (1) Asnova pursues the aim of uniting Rationalist architects, and workers of a like mind in the field of architecture and construction, with the intention of raising architecture as an art to a level matching the present state of both technology and science.... (2) In accordance with its aims, the activity of Asnova is divided into (a) work within the Association, where an exchange of information and experience takes place through lectures, papers, debates, seminars, the organization of courses and schools etc; (b) work outside the Association: a consultations concerning various questions of architecture, town planning and construction in general:b the drafting of architectural structural designs, estimates, costings etc: c the directing of architectural building work on site in accordance with the tasks formulated in para. 1 of the Constitution: d the area of activity covers the city of Moscow. . . . (4) The membership of Asnova consists of: a honorary members, b ordinary members, c candidate members. Honorary members are elected through secret ballot by a General Meeting from individuals who may prove useful to Asnova by their activities. . . . Only those individuals may become ordinary members who by their ideology and activity support the Rationalist Architects and possess a scientific qualification equivalent to a completed education at higher technical or art college, and sufficient practical experience. . . . . . . Students attending senior courses at higher educational establishments may only become candidate members. ... . . . Students in their last year at higher educational establishments may become ordinary members of Asnova. . .. The activities of Asnova are managed by: a the general meeting of members, b the Architectural Council, e the Directorate, d the Auditing Commission. . . . The general meeting consists of honorary, ordinary and candidate members. . . . The Architectural Council consists of the members of Asnova. To conduct its activities, the Architectural Council elects a Directorate consisting of: the chairman, two of his colleagues and a secretary. (From Nikolsky's archive) Extracts from the slogans and statements published in Izvestiya Asnova, 1926 The USSR is the builder of a new way of life. The USSR is the collector of class energy for the building of a classless society. The USSR also sets new tasks for architecture. The USSR represents rationalized labour in unity with science and highly developed technology. Asnova considers that its foundation is the material embodiment in architecture of the principles of the USSR. Asnova considers it urgent that architecture should be armed with the latest equipment and methods of modern science. Asnova considers that in order to move modern architecture forward, the present, strategic moment requires mutually creative labour on the part of architectural producers on the one hand and the labouring masses of consumers on the other. The practical work of today will culminate in a theoretical system tomorrow. Asnova, bearing in mind the rate of modern invention, when every passing day confronts the architect with new technological organisms, considers that in the existing situation it is most important to establish generally applicable principles in architecture and rid it of atrophying forms. Asnova is working on the establishment of a precise and scientific terminology in contemporary architecture, as it considers that this would offer substantial means for its improvement. For whom is Asnova working? Asnova is working for the mass which demands: a standard architecture, on a par with a motorcar or a shoe; b more from the habitat than the bare equipment required for the fulfilment of natural needs; e from architecture, a rational justification of its features; d craftsmanship, not dilettantism. Who works in Asnova? All architects who recognize their responsibility towards the increasing volume of construction, which is threatened by anarchy. All engineers who require technical structures which come up to the level of art. All builders of all that is new, whose paths cross that of architecture.' (Izvestiya Asnova, Moscow, 1926, p. 1) Extracts from the Programmatic-Ideological Position of the Asnova Sector’, 1931 . . . The architecture of the epoch of proletarian dictatorship must be a powerful productive-economic and cultural-ideological factor in the building of socialism. . . . By actively revolutionizing and shaping the psychology and ideology of the proletarian masses, architecture must intensify their creative initiative and activity on the path to socialist construction by creating an environment favourable to creative activity. Such a view of architecture obliges one, when engaged in the resolution of concrete tasks of socialist construction, not to be content to serve the process of production and ordinary life by elementary planning in space, and not to allow material spatial form to serve in a purely passive way, but to create – by the full conjunction of artistic and spatially plastic qualities and images – a powerful organizing stimulus for the ideology of the masses. . . . . . Proletarian architecture, when it is resolving the tasks set by the reconstruction of productivity and everyday life, must provide a dialectical synthesis of the economic, technical, plastic and ideological factors. . . . Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos . . . The plastic and ideological expressiveness of architecture should not be regarded as an external addition, an ornament or a luxury which may or may not be permissible depending on economic circumstances: proletarian architecture must be penetrated through and through by plastic and ideological expressiveness in all conditions and in every concrete situation; its plastic and ideological effect must be organically grounded in the conjunction of the essential parts of an architectural whole. No quality is absolute, no model is supreme, and no standard achieves utmost excellence, but the highest quality must be attained by a perfect organization of all that the architecture conveys in the given concrete conditions and situation. . . . as part of the resolution of any concrete task, the class ideological function of architecture must be organically incorporated on equal terms into the complex of conditions determining the architectural form. ## The features of proletarian architecture Socialist planning determines the completeness of the dialectical synthesis in terms of economics, technology, plasticity, and ideology. In its turn, this synthesis determines the highest degree of integration of all the elements of proletarian architecture. The plastic expression of this general unity is produced by the following considerations: (1) the unity of plastic form and productive functions, i.e. form rationally determines the productive or domestic process, organically corresponds to it and supports it psychologically; (2) the unity of the plastic features of form and the technological construction from which the construction gains expression. Theconstruction becomes a factor of a powerful class ideological impact; (3) the plastic unity of all the elements of the architectural ensemble, from individual structures to,the complex of a socialist city; (4) an active plastic connection between architectural form and its environment (architecture and nature); (5) the organic conformity of the architectural form's plastic features with the form of pedestrian traffic passing by and the nature ofindividual perception of architectural form, as well as the position and role of such form within the general system of the architectural ensemble concerned. This imposes the obligation of adopting a particular type of composition as a solution based on principle in each actual case; (6) dynamism as a plastic feature, whereby the higher degrees ofideological expressiveness can be achieved and active orientation can be imparted to an architectural complex; (7) tension as the apex of plastic activity, to stimulate creative activity and arouse a feeling of cheerfulness, clarity, assurance, strength and joy; (8) deliberate asymmetry as a principle of composition, on the strength of which the supreme unity of a complex of architectural objects can be achieved in full accordance with the impetus and scale of the processes of socialist production; (9) a deliberate organic synthesis of architecture with figurative sculpture and painting, aimed at intensifying the plastic and ideological power of expression of architecture, so as to fill it with images of revolutionary struggle and construction. The degree of principle and fullness of treatment supplied by the set of features enumerated above, together with the clarity, novelty, grandeur and wisdom of the compositional approach, determine the fullness and force of the proletarian architecture's ideological power of expression.’ (Sovetskaya arkhitektura, 1931–32, Nos. 1–2, pp.46–47) ## Osa: Union of Contemporary Architects Osa was founded at the end of 1925, with Alexander Vesnin as chairman, Ginzburg and Viktor Vesnin as deputy chairmen, and Orlov assecretary. The membership of Osa included Barshch, Andrei Burov, Vegman, Leonid Vesnin, Vladimirov, Gan, Ilya Golosov, Nikolai Krasilnikov, Komarova, Leonidov, Loleit, Nikolsky, Nikolaev, Nikolai Sokolov, Sobolev, Fisenko, Kholostenko, Khiger, Yalovkin and others. The periodical Sovremennaya arkhitektura was published by Osa from 1926 to 1930, with Alexander Vesnin and Ginzburg as editors. The First Osa Conference was held in April 1928. The First Osa Congress was held in May 1929. In 1930, Osa became a sector of Movano, was reorganized as Sass – Sektor Arkhitektorov Sotsialisticheskogo Stroitelstva (Sector of Architects for Socialist Construction) – and was dissolved in 1932. Extracts from the statement of the Osa group to the Administrative Section of the Moscow Soviet, February 1926 . . . the proposed organization, as distinct from those already in existence, will unite architects and production workers, with whom they are linked, on the Left Front of architecture. The Union proposes to resolve and implement collectively, with constructive clarity, logical simplicity and functional relevance, all the architectural constructional problems arising from the present times, the features of socialist construction in the Republic and the general tempo of the country's productive life. .. the Union of Contemporary Architects proposes to include in its membership not only professional architects, but also individuals closely connected with building production and united with us by a common ideology. . . .’ (Iz istorii sovetskoi arkhitektury 1926–1932 gg. Dokumenty i materialy, Moscow, 1970, p.68) Extract from Osa's statement to the Art Department, Chief Administration for Science, People’s Commissariat for Education, 1926 . . . we think it necessary to point to the following substantial differences. The Moscow Architectural Society includes a large number of architects with diverse ideologies and, for this reason, the ideology of modern architecture essential for the expression of socialist tasks cannot be formulated within it with sufficient clarity. Osa brings together people closely bound by a single ideology and conducts collective-theoretical scientific research and practicalwork on a well-defined plane in a struggle against inertia and survivals from the past. Asnova is the union of a small group of architects with a definite ideology, the essence of which is the quest for an abstract form in purely aesthetic terms. Osa collectively resolves, and implements in practice, the new architectural form functionally derived from the purpose of a given structure, its materials, construction and other production conditions, and thereby responds to the concrete tasks set by the socialist development of the country.' (Iz istori sovetskoi arkhitektury . . , op. cit., p.69) Chapter 3/Architectural associations of the new direction Editorial on The Tenth Anniversary of October’, 1927 There is no other field of artistic labour in which the events of October produced as decisive, as categorical a revolutionary change as in architecture. The October Revolution embodied the significance of a new historical phenomenon of unprecedented sweep and scale and was destined to demolish by its very nature the outdated principles of pre-Revolutionary architecture. Once it had abolished private property, the Revolution threw open to Soviet architects prospects of grandiose planned work, work on the formulation of new models, new architectural organisms, new complexes and sets of buildings, instead of the narrowly individualistic tasks dictated by the pre-Revolutionary customer. The abolition of private ownership ofland provided the widest possible opportunities for the new planning of cities and settlements, while the generally positive and creative trends introduced by October brought forth the idea of Constructivism as an idea of life-building and life-organizing labour, in place of the ornamental and decorative varieties of old art. The first phase in the development of architectural Constructivism was negative, in line with the initial stage of post-October culture as a whole. Its cutting edge wasset to the struggle against layers of inertia and atavism, and to the destruction of surviving traditions, whose tendrils were greedily grasping at the shoots of innovative thought. The second, positive phase of Constructivism – in parallel with the economic growth of the Soviet Union and the constructive phase of our entire national economy – has already clearly left its mark on the movement's creative activity, by clarifying for it the purposive involvement of the Soviet architect in the establishment of the social condensers of our era, an involvement which sets our Constructivism apart from all Leftist trends and groupings in Western Europe and America. In step with the sweeping increase in the rate of construction work in the Soviet Union, Constructivism is opening up for itself, on the tenth anniversary of October, prospects of strenuous work in connection with the creation of a high level of new architectural culture on the basis of this socio-public purposefulness, covering in equal measure questions of art and questions of utilitarian technology, so as to solve them monistically, by the total fusion of new content and new form. This tenth anniversary must become Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos the date marking the complete liquidation of pre-Revolutionary eclecticism and retrospectivism, the date of the intensive development of genuinely new construction which will make use of all the grandiose opportunities that the October Revolution offers it.’ (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1927, Nos. 4–5, p. 111) Extracts from the resolutions concerning the reports of the Ideological Section adopted at the First Osa Conference, April 1928 The First Conference of the Union of Contemporary Architects stresses its complete unanimity and adopts the ideological position and production programme of Constructivism in architecture. Constructivism evolves methodological ways and means of social-artistic labour, and creates new manifestations ofit to counter aspects of idealistic art surviving from the past and prevailing even now. . . . we, as Constructivists, contrast the passive execution of a commission on the basis of old pre-October types of architecture with the study of the socially domestic and productive mutual relationships of the post-October era, and of their active embodiment in a series of new types of architecture on the basis of comprehensively planned socialist construction. We set the new type of communal dwelling, the new type of club, Palace of Labour, Executive Committee building, factory etc, which are to become the conductors and condensers of socialist culture, against the pre-Revolutionary types of the “investment property", the private house, the“Noble Assembly" building etc, which resulted from pre-Revolutionary social, economic and technological conditions, but serve to this day as a basis for the architecture that is being erected in the USSR.
