Against the passive use of old, archaic materials, structures and methods of construction, which arose in the circumstances of a socio-economic system that is now defunct in our terms; which werethe heritage of our pre-Revolutionary destitution and inertia and which now hamper the rate and quality of the new socialist culture's growth, we set the determined conquest of our backwardness; the active and scientific acquisition of all the achievements of worldwide technology in the field of the latest materials, structures, mechanization and standardization of building production; and the plannedintroduction of all these achievements into our everyday building practice, due account being taken of the USSR's special economic features. In matters of form, we equally categorically reject: (1) the ignorant attitude of builders and engineers alien to questions of the socially artistic quality of architecture; (2) the unprincipled eclecticism of the“decorators"of architecture, who clothe every kind of architectural social content in the garb of ready-made styles from the past; (3) abstract forays into new form divorced from the social purpose of architecture and any realistic means of execution; (4) naive dilettantism seeking to reflect one or another outlook on the world by means of decorative architectural form; (5) work in the so-called “new style", using the elements of the new architecture as means of“contemporizing" and prettifying essentially outdated structures. We set against all this the organic growth of Soviet architecture, which arises from the specific features peculiar to the new social model and the technically perfected methods of building production. We resolve questions concerned with the effect– ideological, emotional etc- upon the user, not by the addition of decorative elements, but by: (1) the very system governing the structuring of the new social model, the clarity of the articulation of social domestic functions of various kinds and the general sense of purpose of the entire architectural organism; (2) the highest standard of quality of all elements and structural parts without any exception, as required by their social and technical purposes; (3) the employment of all the specific features of various elements of architecture, such as plane, volume, spatial relationship, scale, texture, light etc, while treating one or other of their properties, not as a self-sufficient abstract value, but as a continuously variable quantity, resolved anew in each case, depending on one or other of the conditions governingthe aim, purpose and concrete possibilities of execution involved. Against the work by architects in the National Republics of the Soviet Union which amounts to resurrecting the national styles of the old bourgeois culture, we advance work that is directed at creating a socially new architecture, taking account of the national habits of life, and climatic, technical and economic conditions in the individual republics, on the basis of a radical transformation of social class relationships. As to questions of new architecture in the West, we note elements of kinship between our work and the area of activity of the most progressive architects of the West in which the highest achievements of world science and technology have taken effect, and our complete divergence with regard to the purposive approach of our architecture, a divergence which manifests most vividly the whole difference between the bourgeois social structure of the capitalist West and the new social relationships in a proletarian country engaged in building socialism.' (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1928, No.3, p.78) Resolution concerning the report of the Osa Section for the Planning of Housing, 1928 ‘The First Conference of the Union of Contemporary Architects takes note of the imperative need in connection with the design and construction of new housing to change from individual flats to new communal dwellings with a clear distribution of individual and communalized functions, aimed at the greatest possible communalization and collectivization of the processes of everyday life. In the course of this work, the new architect must take close account of the need to raise the social quality of the housing intended to promote the organization and progress of the new way of live, but also of the need for the lowest possible cost of housing, its further reduction by all available means, and the improvement of its efficiency. In terms of the planning of populated areas, we set against the chaotic, random building work in cities, and against the act of planning in accordance with predetermined schemes derived from a graphic starting point, or from long since outdated ways of life, the thorough study of a city's functions, not in their static condition, but in terms of a city in motion, in its dialectic, taking account of its maximum possibilities of further development. We contemplate the planning of a new city only by taking account of all the factors bearing on a city's character and role, of the commitment to the socialist reconstruction of the country and of work on the selection of materials and structures that will ensure elasticity and the possibility of the most painless changes in planning both of the city as a whole and of individual buildings. (Sovremennaya arkhitektura, 1928, No.4, p. 123) Chapter 3/Architectural associations of the new direction Extracts from the Sass theses At a New Stage, 1931 . . . (2) The Osa slogans were directed along four lines: a Creative-methodological; b Design; c Constructional-technological; d Socialist settlement. The six years during which Osa has been in existence have tested the viability of these slogans and shown what was correct, and what was incorrect in them. . . . (4) It is however as clear to Osa as ever that an architectural construction must result from the study of the processes of everyday life; social and production processes; the technological and static properties of building materials; the means of building production in the context of the economy of the reconstruction period; and the socialist reorganization of production and the way of life in the given economy. The new content of architecture must derive from and be determined by the socialist reorganization of production, culture and the way of life. The new form of architecture, being the outcome of the spatial reorganization of social processes, must derive from and be determined by a switch from the existing method of building to a standardization and typification of buildings which would make it possible to go over from seasonal construction work to mechanized assembly all year round. . . . (5) Osa considers that the slogan of the Party and working class: “To catch up with and overtake in a technological-economic respect the leading capitalist countries" can only be fulfilled given the greatest possible use of engineering in architecture and the provision of a modern scientific technological basis for the latter. . . . (6)Osa is against the view of architecture as “an art of images" propagated by Vopra and AKhR; Osa is for architecture as organized scientific activity. . . . (8) In the field of architectural design, Osa has fought, on the basis of the Functional Method, for socially new types of buildings, for new economic principles governing design in all aspects of building work and for the most rapid possible reconstruction of the way of life in accordance with socialist principles: a for an economic dwelling corresponding to the new socialist conditions and forms of labour, leisure and everyday life; Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos b for new public buildings maximally conducive to the organization and intensification of the activity of the labouring masses and their level of culture; c for a socialist type of industrial building that will provide the workers with the maximum facilities in both production and sanitary respects, and the proletariat with the maximum scope for creativity, initiative and self-help; d for the socialist planning of population settlement on the basis of Marxist-Leninist theory. . . . (14) Osa, which regards questions of socialist settlement as fundamental in the conditions of the reconstruction period, advances the following propositions. During the pre-reconstruction period, when it was a matter of re-establishing the wrecked economy, it was obviously not possible to raise questions concerning a new location for industry, or socialist settlement, or socialist aspects of economy and service in general . . . (15) the reconstruction period set entirely new tasks to the architect intent on fighting for the building of socialism. . . . The task had now become no less than the creation of an integrated system of socialist settlement which would contribute to the greatest possible development of the productive forces of society – to the building of socialism . .. (36) the basic feature of the socialist economic servicing of dwellings is the network, district, non-domestic character of such service . .. (38) all aspects of economic and cultural supply must, of course, be included in the system of public service networks within an area. The following are essential: a road and transport network; b communications network – post, telegraph and radio; c network of dwellings; d food supply network; e public health network; f sewage network; g consumer goods distribution network; h socialist education network– childrens’ and pre-school services; i network of polytechnical eduaction; j cultural and socio-political service network; k physical training and tourist service network; 1 medical service network – surgeries, hospitals, sanatoriums and spas. A fully developed system of socialist settlement implies the creation and full operation of the entire system of network services . . . (48) In all these links of the habitat system, the basic and first task of socialist settlement will be a drive for the complete elimination of individual private domestic arrangements and servicing, as being the most backward and primitive – and hence an unproductive waste of human energy. The socialist dwelling may remain an individual dwelling, but it must not represent an individual domestic economy.' (Sovetskaya arkhitektura, 1931, Nos. 1–2, pp.97–101) ## ARU: Union of Architect-Planners The first organizational meeting of ARU at which it adopted its First Declaration, was held on 7 November 1928, with Ladovsky as chairman, plus Fridman, Grinberg and Glushchenko. When published, the Declaration listed as founders of ARU: Ladovsky, Fridman, Zazersky, Vitaly Lavrov, Glushchenko, Grinberg, Krutikov, Sergei Lopatin, Saishnikov, Sakulin, the public health physicians Nekrasov and Kovalev, and the economist Zhmudsky. Other members of ARU were: Barutchev, Gilter, Grinshpun, Iosif Dlugach, Evgeny Iokheles, Kalmykov, Kaplun, Meerzon, Osipov, Valentin Popov, Rubanchik, Alexander Shevtsov and others. According to the ARU Constitution, members of the group had to have a higher education course in architecture or construction to their credit. A temporary Presidium of ARU, with Ladovsky as chairman and Vitaly Lavrov as secretary, was formed in December 1928, and Rules for Conducting Work in ARU on the Drafting of Commissioned Designs and Participation in Paid and Unpaid Competitions’ – which provided, among all else, for internal competitions to develop design ideas – were also drawn up then. The Permanent Directorate of ARU was elected in October 1929 with Ladovsky as chairman, and Fridman and Zazersky, as well as Vitaly Lavrov from April 1930 onwards, as his deputies. From August 1930, ARU became a sector of Movano. The Second Declaration of ARU was adopted and published in 1931. There was also an ARU group in Leningrad. Along with all other groupings, ARU was dissolved in 1932. Extracts from the First Declaration of ARU, 1928 (1) The general rate of socialist construction and economic development in our country is beginning to have an increasing effect on the growth of towns. The essential characteristic determining the character of the modern town is the intensive growth of its dynamism. . . . (2) The basic features of a town in the socialist system, which differentiate it from the theoretical and practical conceptions chiefly current in the West, derive from the determination to destroy all social inequality within the population; the simplification and ultimate withering away of the class structure of society; the nationalization of land and the elimination in this connection of land rents and opportunities for speculation in land, all of which throw open a broad path to the rational replanning and development of existing towns. . . . (3) In connection with this, it has now become necessary to pay particular attention to the architectural side of new construction and development. . . . Modern man is consistently obliged to operate within an architectural environment. The architectural structures of a city, quite freely perceived, will have a direct impact on the feelings of a “consumer" of architecture and call forth specific perceptions of the surrounding world by their aspect and forms. The Soviet State, which puts planned regulation first among its activities, must use such architecture as a powerful means for organizing the psyche of the masses. A definite direction must be imparted to the impact of architectural objects from the very start in planning inhabited localities. (4) Architecture, understood as a single spatial continuum, must resolve not merely the task of designing individual structures, but connect all groups of structures into a single spatial system within which the individual structures represent no more than parts of a more general architectural entity. Such an interpretation of town planning provides the only reliable starting point for dealing with architectural tasks as systems designed to produce a psychological and ideological impact on the social unity of a city. These considerations make it necessarv, when defining a particular architectural task for the design of an individual structure, first to resolve the more general task of architecturally systematizing the urban entity. (5) . . .The Union of Architect-Planners (ARU) sets itself the task Chapter 3/Architectural associations of the new direction of stimulating social concern for the problems of planning cities. . . . (6) . . . ARU considers it essential to put forward the following initial questions for comprehensive theoretical and practical study: a the planning and architectural design of cities as a social and psychological factor in the education of the masses; b the planning and architectural design of cities as the prime organizational form of bringing architecture to life, in connection with the elaboration of modern principles for the regulation of form in architectural planning; c the general concept of the spatial design of a city and its linkage with the architecture of individual structures; d the need to create a specialized higher educational establishment for the study of the entire body of questions connected with town planning, and a comprehensive statement of aim for the organization of a city in a socialist system; e the development and organization of existing courses concerned with town planning in specialized higher educational establishments and the introduction of a course on urbanism in non-specialist establishments; f the creation of a special organ for the statement and formulation of theoretical problems and for information concerning practical town-planning work in the USSR, Western Europe and America." (Arkhitektura i Vkhutein [Architecture and Vkhutein], First Issue, January 1929, p.8) ## Extracts from the Second Declaration of ARU, 1931 (1) . . . In the conditions of socialist construction, the work of architectural organizations which treat individual structures as parts of a whole is appropriate. ARU is the first architectural union organized in accordance with this principle. (2) . . . The correct resolution of modern tasks of socialist settlement can be achieved on the basis of the scientific study of questions connected with urban construction. (4) ARU regards town planning as a scientific discipline the aim of which is to discover the general rules governing the organization of human settlements in the different forms and developments they adopt, and to establish laws on the strength of which planned forecasting of the processes leading to their formation would become possible, and the random nature of this Part III/Masters and trends: biographies, statements, manifestos process would be overcome, i.e. it would be switched to the rails of socialist planning. . . . A definition of the paths of development of socialist settlement must be based on a dialectic consideration of the factors acting as town-generating principles, while differentiating between these factors in the light of conditions operating at each particular place and time. . . . (5) ARU regards as incorrect attempts to define the development of a city in terms of the application and implementation of a single organizational form of settlement, since a solution of the problem of socialist settlements cannot be based exclusively on a single particular principle. ARU considers it essential to differentiate between varieties of settlements by highlighting the specific features peculiar to a given variety and to proceed from the combination of factors that determine each individual variety of settlement ARU stresses the differential significance ofinhabited localities and the need for a differentiated approach to the stimuli and opportunities for their development; and considers that it is necessary to create organisms that are flexible in social and spatial terms, capable of evolutionary growth within the limits of the given social system and capable of orderly reconstruction. In accordance with this stated position, it is opportune to raise the question of the typological classification of inhabited localities. On its own, the typification and fabrication of single dwellings, or even separate dwelling complexes or districts, carried out in a number of design and construction organizations, cannot bring about a radical solution of the question of the rationalizationof planning, in view of the flawed character of the method applied to the work. According to this method, the question is resolved starting from the particular – a house taken in isolation, or a district – and proceeding to the general – an inhabited location – no account being taken of the qualitative differences in the structure of such an inhabited locality, which depend upon the number of elements of which the locality is composed. (6) The Soviet State, which sets planned regulation as the chief cornerstone of its activity, must also use architecture as a powerful means for the psycho-ideological organization of the masses. . .. (7) . . . ARU considers that among the questions which an architect has to resolve in a particular order of priority and sequence, first place must be given to the social human being in his full, modern, class concreteness. One may provisionally differentiate, within the whole set of questions which an architectplanner is called upon to consider, between those concerning spatial organization, and those concerning spatial composition. It has to be stressed thatthe first category of questions need not be resolved by the architect actingas a specialist, while the participation of an architect is essential in the case of the second category of questions. The degree of priority of these two categories varies in each actual case. In one case, questions of the organization of space may be placed first. In another, questions relevant to the second category may be of decisive significance. But in either case, both these principles must be considered as part of a complex and not in isolation.’ (Sovetskaya arkhitektura, 1931, Nos. 1–2, p.19–20) ## Vopra: All-Union Proletarian Architects' Association Vopra was organized in 1929 and published a declaration signed by, as founder members, Alabyan, Dmitry Babenkov, Baburov, Vlasov, Elegont Deryabin,Nikolai Zapletin, Zaslavsky, Zilbert,Ivanov, Kozelkov, Kochar, Alexander Krestin, Kryukov, Mikhail Kupovsky, Mazmanyan, Matsa, Mikhailov, Mordvinov, Polyakov, Terekhin, Simbirtsev, Grigory Solodovnik and Avraam Faifel. Its directorate comprised Matsa as chairman, Zaslavsky as deputy chairman, Baburov as secretary, as well as Kozelkov, Mordvinov, Simbirtsev and Deryabin. Vopra's largest organization was located in Moscow, where it numbered forty-nine members in 1930, while local branches were set up in the Ukraine, Georgia, Armenia, Leningrad and Tomsk. Vopra teams took part in many open competitions during the period 1930–31. Documents emanating fromVopra sharply criticized the Osa Constructivists, the Asnova Rationalist Formalists’ and the eclecticists and stylizers in Mao, all of whose work was regarded as failing to meet the class requirements of the proletariat. Vopra was dissolved in 1932. Extracts from the Vopra Declaration, 1929 At all stages of class society, architecture has served the interests of the ruling classes. . . . The architecture of the capitalist era is characterized by decorativeness and a recourse to complacent formalism and technicism. In the USSR, in conditions of proletarian revolution and the building of socialism, architecture still remains in the groove of bourgeois art: Constructivism, Formalism and, especially, eclecticism are currently the dominating tendencies in architecture. These trends, identical with the same trends in the capitalist West, have altered their outward appearance and adapted themselves to our circumstances under the impact of our reality, but have remained essentially alien to it. At the same time, proletarian architecture is arising from the economic foundations of the transitional period. . . . We reject eclectic architecture and the methods of the eclectics, who mechanically copy the old architecture and blindly subject themselves to Classical canons and schemas. Eclecticism was especially widespread in the architecture of the period of mercantile industrial capital. . . . We reject Formalism in architecture, which made its appearance during the period of developed industrial capitalism. . . . Formalism was the consequence ofa collision between the psycho-ideology and habits of the petit-bourgeois intelligentsia with the monopolistic tendencies of the period of financial capitalism. . .. We recognize the positive historical role of Constructivism (as indeed of Formalism) in the process of overcoming eclecticism and the mindless application of technological routine, in the promotion of questions concerning the rationalization, mechanization and standardization of construction, in propagandizing industrial technology etc. But we note at the same time that, in the conditions prevailing in the USSR, though Constructivism achieved positive results in terms of the critique of eclecticism and pre-industrial forms of architecture, it has nevertheless proved unable in its own theory and practice to go beyond mere Leftist mouthing and “revolutionary" clichés. We reject Constructivism, which has arisen on the basis of fi- Chapter 3/Architectural associations of the new direction nancial capital. The fundamental features of monopolistic capitalism – a bias in favour of capitalist planned arrangements, rationalization and powerful industrialization – have determined the nature of this architecture. . . . We reject every sort of attempt to blur the class role of architecture and to saddle the proletariat with an architecture unconnected with class, such as could only be realized in the conditions of a communist society. We consider that in the period of the dictatorship of the proletariat and the struggle for the socialist rearrangement of the world, architecture must belong to class in its content and form, that it must . . . participate in the class struggle on the side of the fighting proletariat by all the means at its disposal. We consider that proletarian architecture must develop its theory and practice on the basis of an application of the method of dialectical materialism. . . . We are in favour of a proletarian class architecture, of a constructively and formally integrated art which shows vitality and organizes the will of the masses for struggle and labour. We are against a divorce of form from content and form from construction: we are in favour of their organic unity. For us, form is not a canon, nor an abstract symbol or construction – not an aim in itself, but the means of expressing a concrete significance. An architect must, however, achieve formal, compositional and constructional mastery. We are in favour of assimilation of the culture of the past, but on the basis of Marxist methods of analysis and of the critical use of historiçal experience in the process of creating a proletarian architecture; not its imitation or mechanical copying as in eclecticism. We are in favour of the dialectical use of historial experience in the process ofcreating a proletarian architecture. We are in favour of a dialectial use and application to architectural creativity of all achievements of modern science bearing on form, colour etc. And so our method in architecture is based upon a comprehensive view of the object of architecture, upon taking account of the largest possible number of elements which make up architecture in their interconnections, contradictions and reciprocal effects upon each other, in social-economic, emotionalideological, constructive-technical and other terms.' (Stroitelstvo Moskvy, 1929, No.8, pp.25–26) Bibliography ## Abbreviations M. = . = . = “” = “ ” () “” = “ ” ( “ = () " = “ ) “”= “ ” ( “” = “ “” = “ ” () “ = “ ” ( “” = “ ” (- ” = “ (-) “." = "” () “ = “ () “” = “” (, ) “ = " () =  () “ = “ ” () “” = “ ”( “” = “ ” ( ” = “ () “” = “” () “” = “ - () =   ( “. ” = “ ” (, “ = “ ”( "” = “ ”() “” = “”() “” = “ ” () " = “ () “” = “ () “” = “ ” () "”= " () “” = “- "() “” = “ ” () ## Publications in Russian .  . “, 4, 1964. . (ed) Tatlin, with articles by Simonov, Strigalev, Sarabianov, Zhadova, Kostin, Sirkina; Corvina Kiadó, Budapest 1984; English translation, Thames and Hudson, London 1987 The following abbreviations have been used: arch. – architeet (This concept covers the following diploma classifications: architect, architect-artist and civil engineer) art. – artist eng. – engineer eng.-art. – engineer-artist grad. – graduate AKh – Academy of Arts Api – Azerbaidzhan Polytechnical Institute (Architecture and Building Faculty, founded in 1920) Egu – Erevan State University (included an Architecture Section in its Technical Faculty) First SGKhM – First Free State Art Studios in Moscow (formed in 1918 from the Stroganov Industrial Art College – Skhpu) Ivpi – Ivanovo-Voznesensk Polytechnical Institute (formed in 1918 from the Riga Polytechnical Institute – RPI – evacuated to Moscow) Khisi – Kharkov Institute of Constructional Engineers (founded in 1930) Khkhi – Kharkov Art Institute (formed in 1927 from the Kharkov Art Technical College – Khkht) Khkht – Kharkov Art Technical College (formed in 1921 from the Kharkov Art College) Kkhi – Kiev Art Institute (Architecture Faculty founded in 1924) Lakh – Leningrad Academy of Arts (formerly Petrograd Academy of Arts – Pakh) Ligi – Leningrad Institute of Civil Engineers (formerly Petrograd Institute of Civil Engineers – Pigi) Liiks-Leningrad Institute of Communal-Construction Engineers (formed from Ligi in 1931) Mai (Markhi) – Moscow Architectural Institute (formed in 1933 from Vasi) MGU – Moscow State University Migi – Moscow Institute of Civil Engineers (formed in 1922 from the Moscow Polytechnical Institute – MPI) MPI–Moscow Polytechnical Institute (formed in 1918 from the Polytechnical Institute for Women) Muzhvz – Moscow College of Painting, Sculpture, and Architecture (founded in 1832 and formed in 1918 into the Second Free State Art Studios– SGKhM) MVTU – Moscow Higher Technical Institute (Architecture Faculty) Oiii – Odessa Institute for Figurative Art (formed from the Odessa Art Institute) Okhi – Odessa Art Institute (formed in 1922 from the Higher Art College – Okhu) Okhu – Odessa Art College, founded in 1889, 1918–20 known as the Higher Art College Pakh – Petersburg (later Petrograd) Academy of Arts (founded in 1757) PI – Polytechnical Institute Pigi – Petersburg (later Petrograd) Institute of Civil Engineers (founded 1832) Püips – Petersburg Institute of Communications Engineers RPI – Riga Polytechnical Institute (founded in 1896, and included a Building and Architecture Faculty) Second PPI – Second Petrograd Polytechnical Institute Skhpu – Stroganov Industrial Art College, Moscow (founded 1825) Vasí – Higher Institute for Architecture and Construction, Moscow (founded in 1930 on the basis of the Vkhutein Architectural Faculty) Vkhpi – Vitebsk Artistic Practical Institute (formed in 1921–23 from the Vitebsk Art Studios) Vkhutein – Higher State Artistic Technical Institute, Moscow (formed in 1927 from the Higher State Artistic Technical Studios (Vkhutemas) in Moscow) Vkhutemas – Higher State Artistic Technical Studios, Moscow (formed as a result of the merger in 1920 between the First and Second Free State Art Studios - SGKhM) Note: Numbers in italics refer to captions to the illustrations.
